Decision of Government of India i-xv
Court of Inquiry Report on the 1-201

Accident to Indian Airlines Boeing
737 aircraft VT-EFL on 16th August,
1991 near Imphal


MINISTRY OF CIVIL AVIATION & TOURISM

DECISION OF GOVERNMENT OF INDIA ON REPORT OF THE COURT OF INQUIRY
OF THE ACCIDENT TO INDIAN AIRLINES B-737 AIRCRAFT VT-EFL
NEAR IMPHAL ON 16.8.1991:

On 16th August, 1991 an Indian Airlines Boeing-737 aircraft VT-EFL
crashed near Imphal while operating a flight IC-257 from Calcutta to Imphal.
The flight was under the command of Capt. Shekhar Haldar with Capt. D.B.
Roy Choudhury as Co-pilot. There were 69 persons on board including 6 crew
members. All occupants died in the crash.

2. Government of India appointed Shri Justice Umesh C. Banerjee of the
Calcutta High Court to hold a formal investigation into the accident under Rule
75 of the Aircraft Rules, 1937. Shri B.P. Baliga, Formerly Director of Engineer -
ing, Air India and Wg. Cdr. P. Ashoka, Executive Director (Flight Operations &
Safety), Hindustan Aeronautics Limited, Bangalore were appointed as asses-
sors to the Court of Inquiry. The report of the Court of Inquiry was received by
the Government on 30.4.1992.

3. The Court of Inquiry has concluded the ’Probable Cause of the Accident’
as follows :

"The accident occurred by reason of a grave error on the part of the
Pilot-in-Command in not adhering to the operational flight plan and
ILS let down chart and not realising that his early descent to 10,000
feet and turning right for outbound leg without reporting overhead
VOR would result in loss of time reference and as such misplace him
in the hilly terrain. The Pilot-in-Command’s action may have been
influenced by his extreme familiarity with the terrain".

The Court has ruled out sabotage and structural failure as the cause of
the accident. The Court has made 40 findings and 50 recommendations.

4. The Government have accepted the report of the Court of Inquiry along
with the findings and the probable cause of the accident. Comments on each
finding are given at Annexure 'A’.

5. The recommendations made by the Court of Inquiry aim at enhancing

the safety of aircraft operations and are generally accepted for implementation
as indicated in Annexure ‘B' attached.

FINDINGS

The aircraft had valid Certificate of
Airworthiness and was maintained in
accordance with the approved
maintenance schedules. The air-
craft was fully airworthy prior to Its
departure from Calcutta and during
the course of the entire flight.

The flight crew had appropriate and
valid licences to operate the flight.
Both the pilots have been medically
fit throughout their flying career.

The weather at Imphal at the time
of crash was - Visibility 7 Kms.,
Clouds 3/8 800 feet, 5/8 1800 feet,
1/8 cb 3000 feet, 8/8 10,000 feet -
which was above the operator's
weather minima for an ILS app-
roach at Imphal.

The ATCO had passed to the air-
craft the weather prevailing at 0630
UTC but had not passed the
weather of 0700 UTC which was
same as of 0630 UTC except that
1/8 cb 3,000 feet was not prevailing
at 0630 UTC. The cb was however,
iocated in the North-East direction of
the airport whereas the aircraft
approached form South-West di-
rection. The upper wind at 3.1 Kms
(10160 feet) estimated by India
Meteorological Department at 0600
UTC was 120 /10 Knots.

The landing and navigational aids
(ILS, VOR & NDB) at Imphal Aero-
drome were functioning normally on
the day of the accident. Locator
Beacons at ILS markers however,
have not been provided. The last
calibration was effected on 5th
June, 1991 and was valid on the
date of the accident.

July, 1991. The DME, however, was
subsequently installed and made
operational on 29th November, 1991.

Distance Measuring Equipment
(DME) is a helpful navigational aid
but cannot be termed to be an es-
sential navigational aid. Non-avail-
ability of DME at Imphal has not
contributed to the accident.

Installation of Automatic Direction
Finder - AD-200 at Imphal Airport is
not considered necessary on the
threshold of 21st century, more so
by reason of the availability of VOR
which is a modern navigational and
more effective pilot interpreted aid.

At Imphal there is no equipment in-
stalled to provide Runway Visual
Range information which would be
of great assistance to the pilots for
carrying out instrument approach
and landing during unfavourable
weather conditions.

The flight crew did not adhere to
the operational flight plan after
coming overhead Comilla. They did
not go overhead Agartala VOR and
instead flew almost directly to
Imphal from Comilla,

The flight crew gave incorrect call-
out to Agartala ATC that they were
overhead Agartala VOR even
though the aircraft was nowhere
near Agartala VOR,

The flight crew commenced de-
scent nearly 10 minutes before the
top of descent prescribed in the
Operational — flight plan without
realising that the same would result
in a change of Estimated Time of
Artival,

The flight crew requested Imphal
ATC for setting course directly out-
bound for ILS let down, which was
cleared by the ATC. This was pro-
cedurally a wiong call-out by the
flight crew, Permission by the Air
Traffic Controller for direct outbound
could not possibly have misled the
pilot not to come overhead VOR,

The flight crew gave wrong altitude
call-outs to Imphal ATC on a number
of occasions during descent and let
down which can only be termed as
an act of indiscipline.

The aircraft never came overhead
lmphal VOR even though the ATC
had asked it to report overhead.

The flight crew reported approach-
ing overhead VOR even though the
aircraft was about 14 miles away
from Imphal VOR at that point of
time,

The flight crew had initially intimated
to Imphal ATC the estimated time of
arrival as 07:16 hours UTC. How-
ever, after setting course directly to
Imphal from Comilla they revised
their ETA to 07:12 hours UTC.

The possibility of the flight crew
being misled by reason of false Indi-
cation of the VOR is ruled out,

The flight crew by vittue of not
reporting overhead VOR and turn-
ing right much shorter of the same,
instead of turning left overhead VOR
for tear drop entry, lost the time ref-
erence required for carrying out ILS
lef down.

Till the time of impact the aircraft
was not on localizer or glide path.

There was total lack of discipline
and professionalism during the flight
on the part of the flight crew by not
adhering to the operational flight
plan as also the ILS let down chart
together with wrong position reports
and altitude call-outs,

The — Pilot-in-Command = did not
adhere to the ILS let down chart but
instead seems to have relied upon
the ground features with which he
thought he was extremely familiar.

The flight crew committed a grave
error of not realising. that early com-
mencement of descent to 10,000
feet and turn to right without com-
ing overhead VOR would misplace
them in the hilly terrain further away
from the airport.

No emergency condition was com-
municated by the flight crew during
the entire course of flight till the time
of impact.

RECOMMENDATIONS

The Indian Airlines should reiterate
to all its pilots that they must meticu-
lously follow all stipulated opera-
tional procedures.

The Flight Safety Directorate of
Indian Airlines should be restructured
SO as to Introduce a flight standardl-
sation cell which would be responsi-
ble for surveillance checks on board
the aircraft as also simulator training
and monitoring of other related
operational aspects, This should,
however, be in addition to the
routine proficiency checks as js
prevalent,

The operator should cary out ran-
dom checks of Cockpit Voice Recor-
der, Flight Data Recorder and Digital
Flight Data Recorder and the DGCA
should ensure compliance thereof,

The training programme of the
operator ought to inculcate a sense
of cockpit discipline amongst the pl-
lots in order to ensure smooth and
efficient operation of the flight with
meticulous observance of stipulated
procedures.

Indian Airlines training programme
should emphasise the need for cor-
rect call-outs by pilots and under no
circumstances the pilot should in-
dulge in wrong and inappropriate
call-outs.

The Indian Airlines training prog-
ramme ought to provide that the
pilots should be guided by available
navigational aids rather than the
visual ground fixes specially on an
IFR flight.

INTRODUCTION

it has happened again - on Friday the 16th August, L9O9L,
the nation was stunned over the broadcast on AU India Radio and
Doordarshan in regard lo a major air accident wherein there were
no survivors. This time again it is a scheduled flight of a sched-
wed operator - tndian Airlines and the flight being IC-257 from
Calculta to dJnmiphal.

After the Kanishka accident in June L985, the country
breathed a sigh of relief at least for nearly three years but it has
Starled again and in a consistent fashion from August, 1988
ouwards. In July, 1988, Pawan Hans Westland helicopter ona
routine flight from Jammu to Vaishno Devi crashed, in which all
the seven people unfortunately while ona pilgrimagse died. In
August, 1988, the Dauphin helicopter belonging M/s Pawan Hans
crashed near Pondicherry. Two several accidents thereafter in
October, 1988 within a matter of few hours stunned the nation
and the operators were Vayudoot and Indian Airlines. The occur-
rences being at Guwahati and Alumedabad respectively. Again in
September, 1989, a Dornier aireraltl belonging to Vayudoot also
crashed near Pune leaving no survivors. Within a period of three
months there was the Pawan Hans helicopter crash in December,
1989, near Patna. That is not the end of the accidents in our Civil
Aviation sector. In February, 1990, one of the most serious air
accidents of a most modern aircratt (A-320) did lake place at
Bangalore in which 92 persous died though providentially, howev-
er, 54 persons could escape the fatality. The next in the Une is in
August, 1991, at lnphal killing all G9 bersons on board including
the crew.

Considering the above data, there were thus, in fact, eight
accidents in three years between July, 1988, to August, 1991, In
which 363 valuable lives were lost.

The time has, therefore, now come to consider seriously as to
the happenings in Wie Civil Aviation sector in the country anda
proper Introspeclion ought to be effected without losing any further
time - introspection to detail out our manner and method of work-
lng so far as the civil aviation industry is concerned in the couulry.
Courts of Inquiry have been constituted in the past - inquiry re-
ports have also come. I am sure the Government must have also
linpleimented quite a good number of the recommendations of
those Courts of Inquiries, but practicabilily of the situation,
however, demands more and not to be content with the recom-
mendations or implementations thereof. It is at this slage that this
introspection is required - there must be something wrong some-
where as otherwise consistently from August, 1988 lo August,
1991, so many fatal accidents would not have been there. We
should not be content with what we have done but we will have to
cousider as lo what is to be done in the matter of prevention of
future accidents of this nature. Human lives are precious aud
invaluable. Monctary conipensation or a word of sympathy will not
do - we will have to rise up to the occasion to stop the recurrence
of the same, which is otherwise avoidable in nature. The sense of
discipline ought to be inculcated in such a way so as lo restore the
feeling of confidence in our national airline which at present is in a
gradual decline aud I suppose no blame can be attributed to the
travelling community of the society, moreso by reason of the
happenings in the aviation industry of the country in the recent
past as narrated above. Incidentally, it is not out of place to
mention that apart from the incidents noted above, which turned
out to be fatal, there are very many minor ones (not so fatal, but

could have been so) as appears from lic media.

Tam not trying to impute any failure om anyoue but there is
no dental of fact that a feeling of insecurity is gradually gaining
ground in the uiunds of the people as regards the safely of air travel
in this country. I am conscious of the fact that the number of
accidents in our country may not be that much as js in some other
countries but that in my view cannot be a solace - that ought not

lo give a sense of colplacency tn us so that we can take Uiings as
they are and leave it for the future to do the needful. The tine and
the hour has come for us to rise up to the oceasion and do our

utniost to save such fatalities.

The effort of the Government in the matter of prevention of
such fatalities, however, cannot but be appreciated. No sooner the
accident occurred at Imphal, the Minister of Civil Aviation and
Tourism announced over the media that this accident shall be
Investigated bya Judge of the Nigh Court and in pursuance
whereof I was appointed to head the Court of Inqury under Rule
75 of the Aircraft Rules 1937 with two assessors - Shri B.P. Baliga
and Wg. Cdr. Pp, Ashoka, one. belonging to the cnginecertng disei-
pline and the other a very renouned lest Pilot of the country. tn
terms of the notification, the time to complete the inquiry and to
make the report was stated lo be 31st December, 1991, but subse-
quently by two several notifications dated 20th December, 199]
and 31st March, 1992, the Uine to make the report was extended
uplo 30th April, 1992. A copy of the original notification dated
26th September, 1991 is however, annexed to this report for
convenicuce sake and ts darked with letter A.

Furthermore, the subsequent request of the Government to
te Court to visit Australia and Singapore so as to have a comlpara-
live analysis of our Civil aviation system goes to show in my view,
the Slucerily of the Government in the matler of salety of the air
Passengers.

IL is lo be noted that at the request of the Government, I did
visit Sydney so as lo undertake a study of the Civil Aviation Indus-
ty in Australia and on my way back homie | did also visit, though
for a short while, Singapore to appraise myself of the systems
followed by the two inost renouned airlines of the world today,
nainely, the Qantas Alrways and Singapore Alrlines,

Brielly slated it has been a well-worth visit to Australia and
Singapore: How an alrline operator engages ilself in its operational
sphere having due regard to the salely aspect is seen to be be-
Heved. The culture, the system control, the outlook, the sincerity
for the purpose together with the devotion to the concept of air
passengers safety needs Special mention.

As regards Qantas Airlines, the thing which is of utmost
importance is the outlook of Qantas cullure. Actions shall have to
be taken agaiust any iinpropriety or deviation from Qantas culture,
but that should not be punitive in Mature - it is not what has
_ happened but how to prevent the recurreuce of the same - this
particular outlook has brought Qantas the Nae and flame as (hey
are enjoying today throughout the globe. -Another redeeming
fealure of Qantas cullure is the rellauce on to the pilots’ union: (he
Management in Une event of a departure of the flying norms (hougt
takes note of the Situation, but it is the union which, in fact, takes
care of the situation. Confidence on to the union to tus extent,
cannot but demand praise and appreciation. In the event, howev-
er, there is recurrence of such a deviation from the flying norms
more than one occasion, it is the Qantas culture again that would
Pproinpt the Management to consider the matter in its retrospect -
analyse the situation inchiding the training aspect and if tt is so
felt necessary then and in that event the trainer himself gels a
further training to impart training to the flying crew - an unique
effort for safety of air passengers.

Does the Operator in our country goes to this extent that a
small deviaUon needs that much of care and attention - do we have
the time, energy and the inclination - the answer probably be not
In the affirmative. Noleworthy here that itis the operator and not
the regulatory authority which takes this much of pain to study
the entire situation and see that the deviations, however, small in
nature do not recur again - public salety is predonunant in Quan-
tas culture. We do also have in our national airlines a Directorate
of Air Safety, but tunfortunately the Air Safety Directorate does not

even hold a meeting for a Perlod of three years. Can it be said to
be a situauion which is conducive to publle Salely - my answer
unfortunately cannot but be in the uegative.

I cannot but help noUng another aspect even at this Juncture
concerning the executive pilots. As in our system, the executive
Pilois in Qantas Airlines do also fly, As a matter of fact the entire
operallonal Managenient is being aduunistered by the flyers only -
but there exisis a difference and the difference being - whereas the

if not slightly more, Qantas however docs nol allow its exectuive
Pilots to fly more than one-third of what a line pilot does: the
reason is not, however, far to seek - the entire administrative
Super structure consists of the executive pilots and if they fly as
the line Pllots do, where Is the time to devote on to the organisa-
tonal aspect and other adniunistrative works - where ts (he ime to
(hin: and evolve the best Salely methods.

Incidentally, against a pointed question asked by mie to Capt.
Mehta to the Same elfect, the auswer of Capt. Mehta was that
sufficient time is available to look into the administrative and
organisational aspect also - there lies probably the difference + we
take things as they are : we have no intention nor any inclination
to give it a thought about the whole situation. We want lo contin-
ue with the exis ling system as ihey are : altitude of the Australian
Civil Aviation Industry is, however, slightly different - It is a salety-
oriented approach and they evolved the system in such a way so
that the entire system can develop in its proper perspective having
due regard to the Safely aspect. | will be falling in my duty iff do
ol mention, however, here iiself, that all the exceulive pilots are
duly compensated in terms of money and they get a fixed reiu-
neration having due regard to the flying tne with which the execu.
live pilots otherwise would have been associated. The entire super
structure, therefore, of our naUlonal operator needs a change and it
is for the Government and the operator to look into the matter as
otherwise there will only be a compronuse with the Safety of air
Passengers,

4n

The operator's organisational structure needs {to be revamped so
that a cullure is Introduced) which by iiself would nepale any
chance or risk aud will promote safely. The details of the study
undertaken by me at Australia and Singapore will appear in this

ought to exist ouly for the purpose of supervision of the whole

be as safe as in Australia or in Singapore. I ani not prepared to

give ila thought in order to bring home the concept of safety in the

nunds of air passengers which is ina gradual decline at present.

2. THE UNF ORTUNATE FLIGHT

fudian Airlines MUght 1C-257 from Calculta to liophal and
then on to Dimapur was a scheduled Hight of the operator. The
aircraft used was a Bocing 737 having registration number VT-EFL
under the conunaud of Capt. Sekhar Haldar and Capt. D.B. Roy
Choudhury was the Co-pilot. In accordance with usual practice,
Hight bricfing was effected at the Might despatch of the operator
and records to that effect were duly produced before the Court of

pilot and the co-pilot and the usual Boeing 737 flight plan was
made over to the flight crew members which reads as follows: -

As per the flight plan, the aircraft had 7,900 kgs of aviation
fuel though the requirement was of 7785 kgs which takes note of
APU fuel, alternate fuel, holding fuel, Manoeuvre and taxi fuel.
From the actual trim sheet, it appears there was also in fact 7,900
kgs. of fuel on board the aircraft and as such it can sately be
coucluded that the aircraft had sufficient fuel to operate the sector
Calcutta to huphal as a scheduled flight NoIC-257,

Oe

The flight crew detailed to operate the flight consisted of
Capl. Sekhar Haldar as the Commander, Capt. D.B. Roy Choud-
hury as the Co-pilot with four cabin crew members Mrs. Surlya
Sen; Mrs. Sipra Majunidar; Miss Minoti Ray and Miss Sonali Roy.
There were allogether 63 passengers inclusive of one child o
board of which 31 passengers with the child were booked for
huphal and 31 for Dimapur.

The scheduled departure time of the flight from Calcutla was
1130 am. UST) on 16th August, LOOT and the aireratl, tu fact,
taxied out at 11.50 a.m. and it was airborne at 11.54 a.m. from
runway 19L at Calcutta alrport. The ATC tape-trauscript depicts
that inilially the aircraft was cleared to flight level 70 (7,000 feel)
on track and from flight level 70 to flight level 150 (15,000 Icel)
under Radar aud further from: Dhaka Control. The materials ou
record show that the aircraft was cleared to Imphal via route B-593
and W-54 with flight level 290 (29,000 feet} having total flying time
GO minutes for this particular sector (Calcutla-lmphal). The air-
craft in accordance with the usual flight plan did call at Comiulla
and reported “approaching overhead Alpha-Alpha Tango". the
Cockpit Voice Recorder also depicts that the aircraft did contact
abeam Silchar and thereafter caine in contact with Imphal at
12:34:45 hours (IST). At this stage, the aircraft conununicated the
revised ETA to linphal and asked for descent clearance, which was
granted. At 12:36:57 hours (IST) the ATCO at Imphal cleared the
{light IC-257 to descend to 10,000 feet and asked to report over-
head VOR for ILS Runway 04. At 12:39:45 hours (IST) the aircraft
UC-257) Inlinated the Tower that the aircraft is now 12 mules
inbound at 10,000 feet and the Tower at Imphal asked the Pilot
(iC-257) to maintain and report overhead VOR. Imuinediately
thereafter, however, within two seconds the pilot asked the ATCO
as to whether they can set course directly outbound for let down
and the ATCO at Imphal did permit the same. AT 12:41:29 hrs.
(IST) the aircraft transmitted that they are approaching overhead

and three seconds thereafter the Tower transuiilled a message


which reads; “understand you are procecding on a radial 217 for
ILS" and four seconds thereafter the alrerafl replied as alfirmative?
The Tower however imuncdiately asked lo give a call commenuciug

ithe procedure tur.

At 12:42:13 hrs. (IST), the aircraft was asked by hnphal ATC
to proceed outbound for ILS approach for runway 04 descending to
5,000 feet. In the mean time, Imphal ATC allowed IC-890 to enter
runway 04 for take off to Guwahati. Proper separation was given
between IC-890 (departing aircraft) and IC-257 the arriving alr-
craft. This aircraft was also informed about the traffic.

At 12:44:50 lrs. (ST), the aircraft (IC-257) reported com-
mencing procedure turn. However, after departure of IC-890,
Imphal ATC tried to contact [C-257 but there was no response.
After wailing for some Lime aud giving several calls, Imphal ATC
started emergency procedure followed by search and rescue action.

At about 1700 hrs. (IST) bnphal ATC came to know through
police wireless that the aircraft (IC-257) was found to have crashed
ona hill (Thaugjing) at a distance of approximately 20.5 NM on the
approach path of runway 04 of Luphal Aerodrome. The estiniated
coordinates of the crash point are Lat. 24° 29° 54"N_ Long. 93° 40°
20"E. The aircratt crashed at 12:46:23 brs. (IST) under juclement
weather condition having low cloud, occasional rain.

After the impact with the hill the aircraft was coipletely
destroyed. All the G9 persons on board lost their lives. There was
post crash fire and as appears from the materials on record the
wreckage of the ill-fated plane with nose and front section disinte-
graled into pieces and were found South-West of lnphal acro-
drome at a distance of approxiinately 20.5 nautical miles and
about 300 feet below the highest point at that location in the form
roughly of an inverted ’L’. The long arm of inverted "L’ stretched to
approximately 300 to 400 metres and short arm stretched 50 to 75
mictres as per visual estimate. There were burnt patches and the


area looked brown. The direction and spread of the wreckage
indicated a head-ou-crash and a graze on the slope of the hill, The
vegelalion aud trees were either burnt duce to sprinkling of aircralt
{uel and hot debris immediately after the crash or were cut in the
direction of the hill at the time of the crash and was found Huiited
to the crash site. The factum of existence of unburnt material
including currency notes points to a definite direcUioun thal the fire
was after the crash. The damage to the aircraft was lotal by

reason of disintegration duc to the impact with Une hill.

On the basis of the above transcript it therefore appears that
the aircraft VT-EFL (IC-257) from Caleutta to Imphal crashed

3. POST-MORTEM EXAMINATION OF CRASH VICTIMS

1C-257 having taken off from Calcutta on LGU August, 1991,
crashed near lmphal around 12:46 hrs (IST) killing all 69 persons

infant passenger and 6 members of the crew. From the report of

college lead by Dr. L. Femate, Associate Prof. of Forensic Medichic.
Alt the bodies were duly identified and forensic examinations were
had before the post mortem examinations of the bodics. The
evidence on record of Dr. Femate unmistakably suggesis that there
was no chance, considering the injuries on the bodles, of lile,
hninediately after the crash. Dr. Femate also stated that apparent-

neous. According to Dr. Femate the burn injury may be simul{a-

would be capable enough to cause the same.

4. AIRCRAFT INFORMATION :

1) General:

The Boeing 737 Jet Transport is designed to operate over
short-lo-medium ranges. Wing high-lift devices permit operations
from airports not formerly accessible to jet transports. The 737-
200 is 100 fect long and has a 93 feet wlng span. The maxtmum
operaling alr speed is approximately 350 knots EAS or 0.84 Mach.
inaxiinum certiffed altitude Is 35,000 feet.

ip Engine :

The airplane is powered by two Pratt and Whilney JT8bD
turbo fan engines mounted under the wings. The cngine is a
forward-fan-type engine with a twin-spool axial compressor. The
engine has mine can-annular burners in the combustion chamber,
and is provided witha multi-stage reaction-impulse turbine. The
miulll-stage axial compressor cousists of a low pressure unlit, which
includes the fan stages, and a high pressure unit. The low pres-
sure ull is connected by a through-shalt to the turbine wheels for
the low pressure compressor, and the high pressure unit is con-
nected independently by a hollow shaft to the turbine whieel for the
high pressure compressor. ‘The cugine Is cyuipped with a full
length annular fan-discharge duct. A hydromechanical fuel con-
trol governs the rotor speed of the high pressure compressor, aud
schedules [uel {low to provide the thrust called for by the thrust
lever setUng in the cockpit.

The wing mounted engines assures "eye-level maintenance."
The cowl panels hinge at the top of the engines or detach so that
inmost components are accessible without a stand. Engine accesso-
res, plumbing, wiring, ducting, and fire wall attach points for the
(wo engines are identical.

The cngine inlets are forward of dict, dust, and other tor clpn
juatenal alrborne In the wake of the matin landing gear and laps,
The inlet environment of the wing mounted engines provides clean
bleed air for the alrconditioning system. The engines are located
far enough outboard to clear waler or slush spray pattern from the
nose gear. Reverse thrust !s accomplished by two hydraulically
acllvated deflector doors which block engine exhaust pas flow and

deflect the exhaust stream forward.

Engine accessories are easily reached from the ground by
opening linge mounted engine cowlings. The oil tank, fuel/otfl
heal exchanger, and supplementary fuel heaters are integral
cngine parts. Each engine has a 40 KVA geucrator with a constant
speed drive. Pneumatic cugine starters are operated by air sup-
plied through the pneumatic manifold from cither the auxiHary
power uit, the ether engine, or a pneumatic ground cart.

ill) Electrical :

Primary 115 Volt, 400 Hz. AC electrical power is supplied
from a 40 KVA generator located on each engine. The D.C. electri-
cal system is composed of three 50 ampere transformer -rectifier
(TR) wits, a 22 anipere hour battery, and a battery charger. ‘Tlie
APU drives a siugle 40 KVA generator that may be used for main
generator back up power in ght, and cau provide power to all
airplane buses. on the ground.

Pilot electrical load mManagenient has been reduced lo a
minimum. In the event of an engine or generator failure, loads
which are necessary to maintain all primary flight functions are
automatically transferred from the dead bus to the remaining
generator system. This continuous priinary power capability
allows the pilot to continue existing [ight operations until he can
review the electrical panel and take remedial action. Transferred
primary loads will not cause an overload sulllcient to (rip the

2]

remaliug generator. The APU may then be started to assume the

total load of the dead bus.

The DC power distribution system also has automatic trans-
fer to preclude any Interruption of power to the primary radto,
flight instruments or flight controls. Ground operation electrical
requirements can be supplied elther by the APU or from a ground
power supply. In either case, all busses can be powered.

iv) Structures:

The structure of the aircraft is designed to provide maximum
strength with odinimuim welght. The materials most conunontly
used are alununium, steel and Inagnesiuns alloys. Aluminium and
fibre glass honey comb matertal is used extensively. The fusclape
Isa sciulmonocoque structure with (he skin reluiforeed by frames
aud stringers. The fuselage consists of four sections of which the
forward three contain the cockpit, passenger and cargo accommo.
dation whereas the fourth section provides the support to the
cmpennage. The doors are removable units which enclose various
coilmpartiniernts of the acroplane and which pernut cniry to or exit
from those compartments by Passengers, crew or Inaintenance
personnel. The doors are divided for their specific purpose tuto
several groups viz. Passcenger/crew doors; cinergency exils; cargo
doors; service doors; fixed interior doors. An electrical door warn-
{ng system is provided to fudicate to the {light crew that all doors
are closed and properly latched before the flight. The structure of
wing consisis of the left wing box, centre wing box and the right
wing box. Throughout the wing, Wc skin panels are reinforced by
Span-wise stringers and spars. The control surfaces along the
trailing edge of each wing consist of inboard and outboard flaps, an
aileron and a total of four spoilers. The fuel tanks are located in
the wings and are inter-connected. The cinpennage comprises a
dorsal fin, a vertical fin, an adjustable horizontal stabilizer, rudder
and elevators. The structure of the acroplane is protected from

corrosion by application of various finishes.

4B. UTILISATION OF NAVIGATION AND COMMUNICATION AIDS :

Navigational information is derived from (he tollowing sys-

fems :-

(a) ADF System - There are two ADF systems on this aircraft.
ADF-1 and 2 are identical except for the pointer. No.1 ADF
is connected to single bar pointer and.No.2 system ts cor-
nected to double bar pointer in RMI on Pilot and Co-pilots
side. Control boxes are located on the pedestal. The ADF
when tuned to a particular station gives bearing of that

station which is read on the RMI.

(b) VOR System - There are two VOR systems on this aircralt.
Two VOR Nav. Receivers of No.l and 2 systems are located in
the Electric and Electromie (2&E) compartment. The VOR
indications are available on RMI one on each side. These
RMI are conunon for ADF also with the transfer possible wilh
selector. The VOR tnformation is also available on the
Course Deviation Indicator (CDI) one on each side. The VOR
stalion frequency is selected in the control box & audio can
be monitored either from pilot or copilot stalion.

(c) Instrument Landing System (ILS) of B737 alrcratt -
VHF/NAV. receiver No.1! used for VOR No.1, is same for
Localizer No.1, Stuilarly Nav. Receiver No. 2 is common to
Localizer No.2. The Localizer frequency is selected in the
control boxes of No.1 and 2.

Glide Slope signal is also processed in the same VHF Nav.
Receiver unit. Thus Nav. Receivers (Systems No.1 & 2)
process signals for VOR, Localizer, and Glide Slope. If Local-
izer frequency 1s selected in No.1 control box and when
signal strength is sufficient cuough, Che lags of pilots side
CDI will disappear and the vertical bar will deflect ‘OFF’
centre depending upon the position with respeet to the


(d)

centre line of the runway, vertical bar will be at the centre.
In FDI (Fight Direelor Indicator) Runway Symbol also moves
laterally agalust an index mark according to position of
aircraft with reference to the centre Line of the runway.

When the ILS frequency is selected either on No.1 or No.2
control boxes, the Glide Slope receivers will be automatically
‘ON’ (paired frequency) and with the capture of glide path
beam the Nag on FDI and CDI (on both pilot and coptlot side)
will disappear. ‘The horizontal pointer (both on pilot and
copllot side) will deflect off the centre. Pilot follows the bar
and pointer to align the aircraft to the centre line of the
runway and glide path. If etther the ground equipment or
the aircraft equipment is faully the flag will appear on the
CDI (Course Director Indicator) and FDI (Flight Director
Indicator).

The Marker Beacon Receiver (one only) is installed in the
E&E compartment. There are two sets of Marker light indi-
cators consisting of three Ughts on each side installed on
pilot and copiloUs instrument panel. "Blue light is for Quler
Marker, ‘Amber’ for Middle Marker; when the atreralt passes
over (he Markers, Nglits will tuminate on both pilot and
copiloUs side. Simultancously audio siguals will be heard in
loud speaker/Head set.

Weather Radar :- A weather radar system consisting of an
antenna, trausrecelver, control module, and Plan Position
Indicator (PPI) is installed on the airplane. The received
signals are converted to a visual picture on the PPL. The PPI
presentation, of ground and weather returns cnablies the
pilot to navigate ulilizing ground returns, or to detect weath-
er and make a decision to penetrate or circumnavigate the

storm cells.

5 FDR : CVR READ-OUTS:

The report of the luspector of Accidents as also (he evidence
on record disclosed that the Lockheed Flight Data Recorder
(SI.No.555) was retrieved though ina slightly damaged condition at
its rear portion and a missing front main seal from the crash sile
but the engravings on the aluminium foil were otherwise readable
so as lo appreciate the necessary flight path and other incidental
details in regard to the entire flight from Calcutta to hmphal. It is
true that the staUic connector on front face was found broken and
the pitot maUng connector of the alrcralt had sheared off trom
tubing but the recorder could be opened as per the siateinent of
Shri Satendra Singh, Director Alr Safely al DGCA Headquarters
without any difficulty.

The Flight Data Recorder of the ill-fated flight was decoded
and subsequently reviewed in my presence at the Teclinical Cen-
we, DGCA Headquarters, New Delhi as also in the presence of the -
assessors appointed by the Central Government. It was viewed on
the Nikon Profile Projector and all the engravings on the alumiin-
um. foil could be clearly scen. [Incidentally itis to be noted that
Uiis parlicular Lockheed Flight Data Recorder has basically only
five different parameters, namely, (i) Altitude; (1) Heading: (li) Atr-
speed; (iv) Vertical Acceleralion; (v) Time. This particular FDR
seeis to be a very old type of FDR since all modern aircraft have
filled with a Digital Flight Data Recorder having around 300 sever -
al parameters - obviously making the investigation of any air crash
easier, precise and exhaustive. The lack of other parameters
creates some sort of inconvenience and difficullies in the matter of
invesUgation though, however, it cannot be said to be of an impos-
sibility since the difficullles are not insurmountable in nature. [Ht fs
always better to avail of the latest scientific achievement rather
han depending upon an obsolete method in order to arrive ata
correct Hnading.

42


Incidentally, however, il Is to be noled that out of the five
parameters available on an FIR of the Hike nature, this particular
FDR however recorded only four during the entire course of tight
and the vertical acceleration parameter was available ouly for short
futerval during the take off phase from Calcutta. Therefore, this
particular parameter, namely, the vertical acceleration for all prac-
tical purposes ts not available to this Court of Inquiry for the
Inquiry. The non-avlabilily of (his particular parameter may be
due to two reasons (i) The recording stylus of the unit gol adrift as
a resull it was well neigh impossible to have the engravings on the
aluniliiunm foil and () Loss of vertical acceleration data signal due
to mal-functioning of some systems. It is to be noted, however, in
the event of the happening as at (1) above, It will however, leave a
straight line engraving on the aluminium foil-which was, however,
not seen on the foll. In the absence of which one can safely con-
clude that there was no mal-funcliouing of any system of the FDR
unit but the stylus recording the vertical acceleration got adrift
iumediately after the take off from Calcutta Airport. This deficien-
cy of the stylus getting adrift cannot, however, by any stretch be
said to be a mal-functioning of a unit neither it can be ascribed to
be a defective FDR unit at the time of commencement of journey

from Calcutta.

This Court of Inquiry Gaus Is slightly handicapped by reason
of non-availability of (he vertical acceleration of the aircraft.

The decoding of the FDR foil was taken using a lens of 50 X
magnification and the values of the parameters obtained were
checked for their validity and found to be accurate. The details of
the parameters would appear from a schedule Annexed hereto and
marked with letter "C".

flight plan provided by the operator, in this case, the Indian Air-
lines. Subsequent transmission at 06:51:44 from the aircraft to
Agartala Tower to the effect "Roger India 257 approaching overhead
Alpha Alpha Tango’ cannot be said to be a correct transniussion by
reason of the heading, namely, 053 as noted above. On the head-
ing of 053 the aircraft could not possibly have gone overhead Alpha
Alpha Tango. This, therefore, seems to be a wrong call-out and
according to Capt. Mehta, the then Operations Manager of tncdtan
Alrlnes at Calcutta, the aircraft was nowhere near Agartala over-
head and it was a wrong reporting. Capt. Mehta also agreed that
this wrong reporting cannot but be ascribed to be lack of discipline
so far as the pilot was concerned (Capt. Mehta Q. 238 - 241). In
(his context the evidence of Capt. Rahul is of some sipmiffcance: At
first (here was an atlempt on the part of Capt. Rahiut to avoid
answering the question directly as to the factum of the aircraft
being overhead Alpha Alpha Tango by saying that it would be
otherwise tmpossible to be overhead Alpha Alpha Tango since the
obluse angle cannot be availed of by the aircraft and hence there is
a likelihood of violaing the Bangladesh air-space. (Capt. Rahut
Q.19) Subsequently, however, on a pointed question that he being
an expertenced flyer, does he also avoid the fight plan with a
heading of 007 and cut short overhead Agartala by taking 053

direct roule lo Imphal and the answer of Capt. Rahut in no uncer-

.laln terms suggest that he belng ai cxpertenced Hyer would tnvar-

may not be as experienced as him, he should be able to negotiate
the turn froma heading of 007 without any difficulty (vide Q. 248 -

the aircraft is supposed to proceed at a heading of 007 and should
report overhead VEAT t.e. Alpha Alpha Tango (Agartala). ‘This (igtrt
chart has been prepared by the vperator Itself and there ts ample
evidence on record that the chart should be adhered to ln its
observance rather than tn its deviations. Flight charts are pre-
pared having due consideration of all the relevant and necessary
particulars with one object in mind namely the safety of the air-
craft and when the flight chart specifically provides for reporting
overhead VEAT that should imply that this route prescribed is
otherwise safe and in accordance with law without any violation of
any foreign air-space. As such Capt. Rahut’s evidence in regard to
violation of Bangladesh air-space cannot in my view be accepted
and properly appreciated. Obviously Capt. Rahut’s evidence tn
regard thereto namely the violation of Bangladesh air-space was
under mfsconception of the entire situation to say the least about
it. On the subject, still, the evidence of Capt. M.V.V. Rao also
ought to be noted - unfortunately, with Capt. Rao also there was
an alleimpt to avoid the Issue in regard to the adherence of the
flight chart as prepared by the operator - Capt. Rao has stated that
as a matter of fact in the normal circumstances though there
ought not to be any deviation from the chart but deviations can be
had in regard to the altitude. I have not been able to follow this
particular evidence of Capt. Rao. Fught charts as noted above are
to be observed. Question of deviation therefrom does not and
cannot arise. This laxity ought not to be allowed otherwise the
sancllly of the tight chart ts completely obliterated. 1b should have
thought that the head of the training institute of the operator
should be more fair and frank in regard to the failure if any on the

rae

ae

flight chart with the permission of the ATCO, but that can only be
termed to be an exception rather than a regular feature which in
this case as detailed more fully below has happened. I was sur-
priscd that, the evidence of Capt. Rao when he stated before this
Court that as a matter of fact on a plain reading of the CVR he was
exlremely happy as to how a pilot has conducted himself durtag
the course of tlight even though lie had the FDR reading with tifur

inust go deep into the matter - compare the-actual with the CVR

procedure or not. There ought not to be a sense of complacency in

contrary, the first attempt should be to make oul as to whether
there is any failure ou his part so that the lives of the passengers
are not in any way put into any Jeopardy. Human Hives are pre-

6. FLIGHT CREW INFORMATION

a) Capt.Sekhar Halder, Conunander :

Capt. Sekhar Haldar was born on 10 November 1953. He
was issued with ALTP Licence No. 1415 on 11.3.1988. He was
appointed by Indian Airlines on 22 May 1985 and was deputed to
C.T.E., Hyderabad for initial traming and subscquent training on
Boeing 737 aircrall.

His total flying experience was 3783 hrs. out of which flying
expericuce on Boelug 737 aircraft was 2369 hrs. and 1414 hrs tn
other types of aircraft including F-27 aircraft.

After having flown on Boeing 737 as Co-Pilot, he was given

proforma for such a cerlification also contains a column to that
effect. The remark 'No abnormality detected’ (NAD) in my view
cannot be termed to be a sufficient certification more so when in
fact there exists a brealh analyser equipment at the Calcutta
airport. Question of there being an abnormality in a pilot when he
is to report for duty does not and cannot arise. The test is not for
the purpose of delection of any abnormality but for an alcohol
check so as to assure that the pilot has the necessary mental equi-
Hbrium during the fight. The certification, Uherefore, ought to be
in consonance with the object for which such a test is asked to be
had before the flight.

regards the upper wind data for 16th August, 1991, it has been
stated to be at 03 knots at 5,000 feet level at about 12:30 hrs. So
far as 10,000 fect level is concerned, no dala is available, but
considering the ground wind being at 4 knols at that time and 3
knots at 5,000 [cet level it cannot vary to an enormous extent and
itis obviously in the region of less than LO knots - though, howev-
cr, il is a presumption only.

Considering the above, in my view, however, that the upper
wind cannot possibly render any significant change in the flight
path of the aircraft during the lel down procedure. In this context
the evidence of Mr. James Kerrigan, being an Aeronautical Engi-
necr of the Boeing Company, Seattle Washinglon, ought to be
noted. Mr. Kerrigan has categorically stated that in spite ofa
variable wind between 5 to 20 knots, there shall not be much of a
difference in the Might path of the aircraft. Mr. Kerrigan has had
simulator exercises on the basis of this variable wind and pro-

orales the statement as above. For convenience sake, the graph
produced by Mr. Kerrigan is annexed hereto and marked with

stages that some vilal parts are missing. Question of importation

nonns of a commercial transaction and the supplier ought to have
been iitimated in regard thereto iumediately after the supply. For

pilots are never given any primary or secondary area chart, but
while preparing the let down chart some safety zone shall have to
be provided to cater for the error on the part of the pilots so that
the same may provide a cushion or a buffer. It is pertinent to note
that primary area provides for a clearance of 984 feet above the
highest obstruction wilhin the area and if one proceeds (hereafter
towards the ouler boundary of the secondary area, this clearance
of 984 [ect is progressively reduced to zero at the outer extremity of
the secondary area,

From the ILS let down chart it appears that the aircraft after
reporting overhead VOR at 10,000 feet should descend to 8,000

60


feet on the holding pattern itself and then proceed on to the out-
bound leg for 2 minutes 30 seconds from VOR upon a further
descent from 8,000 feet to 5,000 feet at the commencement of ihe
procedure turn with a speed not exceeding 170 nautical miles per
hour. The aircraft whilst on the procedure turn is supposed to
further reduce its height to 4,200 feet and upon capturing the
localiser to proceed inbound. Height of the aircraft shall have to be
al 4,020 feet at the outer marker inbound and then land with (he
assistance of the other landing aids available at the airport. It is
significant to note here that the Pilot-in-Comumand of the aircraft is
not aware of any primary or secondary arca but a strong adher-
ence to the Jet down chart is expected from the pilot. It is in evt-
dence that deviation from the let down chart is nol permitted tn
IFR flight. Safety of the aircraft on the basis of the let down chart
cannot be doubted in any manner whatsoever. Capt. Rahul in his
evidence categorically stated that the let down chart cannot be said
to be unsafe from any point of view. Though it is true that the
chart is not unsafe by any stretch but that does not, however,
mean and imply that ihe bufler or the cushion though intended at
the time of preparation of the let down chart shall not be existing
as a pracUcal reality. The primary and the secondary area oupht,
therefore, to be free of any obstruction or any material obstruction

considering the altitude, the aircraf( ts supposed to be in thal areca.

In the ILS let down chart it has been shown that there exists
an obstruction immediately before Une outer marker inbound ata
level of 3094 feet above sea level and considering the clevation of
the Imphal airport the obsiruction height is (o the extent of about
GOO feet. The ILS let down chart provides the ouler marker in-
bound level at 4,020 feet and considering the ICAO regulation of
obstruction free distance being 984 feet the let down chart can nol
be described to be unsafe in any manner. The importance of
obstruction free primary and secondary area however, can not be
decricd in any way bul since a doubt has been expressed in repard
to the same it would be better for all concerned to review the entire
Situation specially pertaining to tae North Eastern Region Airports

61


and also the hilly terrain airports in the country so as to provide
the necessary buffer to the aircraft in reality rather than in tem-
plates only. I wish to make tt quite clear that Unc let down chart is
quile safe and this is also the considered view of my Assessors, one
being an experienced Mller and other beiug a reputed engineer. The
view as above has also been corroborated by sentor officials of the

operator as well.

The ILS Iet down chart as noted above categorically points
oul that upon obtaining the glide slope at 4,020 feet, the pilot
should follow 035° heading and then land provided, however, the
runway is visible al 224 feet above the runway level. In the event,
however, the runway is not visible at the height mentioned above,
the aircraft is supposed to do a missed approach procedure and
climb straight ahead to 4,200 fect and then turn left to join the
VOR holding pattern al 8,000 feet. Similar provision has been
made in the chart in the event of the glide slope or the middle
marker being not available, the aircraft in Unat event, however,
cannot land unless the runway Is visible from an height of 317 {cet
and the aircraft is to carry a missed approach procedure as noted
above. The procedure laid down is elaborate and if followed ques-
tion of the let down chart being unsafe does not and cannot arise.

The distance between VOR and the outer marker is around 6
nautical miles. From outer marker to the farthest point on proce-
dure turn the distance would be around another 5 nautical miles
and calculating the total distance, therefore, it comes to around 11
nautical miles from VOR. Obstruction if any admittedly is at 17.3
nautical miles at an allitude of 4,265 fect. There is, therefore, a
cushioning area of 6 nautical miles for the aircraft to obtain ILS

assistance and land at Imphal Airport presently.

Mr. Ram has stated that at an indicated specd of 170 knols
and having taken into account statistical wind of 15 knots together
with an additional 10 knots the aircraft can fly otherwise very
safely for 166 seconds outbound from VOR. In accordance with
the chart, the aircraft is not supposed to fly more than 150 sec-
onds. Therefore, total cushioning flying area ts to the extent of 16
scconds, which cannot by any strcteh be termed to be low for sale
distance. As a matter of fact, this cannol be said to be unsate at
all. Thus, from whichever point of view the chart ts looked Upon,
namely, cither from distance-wise or from fiviag: ine-wise there is
sufficient cushioning even at this stage in so far as the let down
chart is concerned.

64

Turning back on to ihe evidence tendered before the Court, i
{s secu that Capt. Rahut himself has subinitted that GO nautical
niles tail wind will drift the aircraft forward maximum to the
extent of 2.5 miles beyond the farthest point of procedure (urn and
in the event of farihest point of procedure turn is treated as 11
nautical miles then in that event at best it can come upto 13.5
nauucal infles aud not beyond that. Assuming a further cushion.
ing zone of 2.5 nautical miles is added thereon, the distance there-
fore comes upto 16 nautical miles. It, therefore, appears that there
ought not to be any difficulty experienced by the pilot even if there
exists a tail wind speed of GO nautical miles provided there is due
adherence to the let down procedure. It is, however, placed on
record that the tail wind of GO nautical miles does not have any
corroborative documentary evidence since the Meteorological
report has recorded maximum upper wind at 5,000 feet as 3 knols
on 16th August, 1991. It is however, placed on record that accord-
tug to the Director Aviation Services, Mr. L.K. Murthy "The esti-
mated 0600 UTC wind on 16.8.1991 at Imphal at 3.1 Kins. is
120/10 kts."

In that view of the matter (he question of existence of ob-
struction in the primary area being the cause of the accident dues
nol arise. The answer to the inquiry nanicly can this aircraft by
reason of 60 nautical miles tail wind drift to the crash site cannot.
therefore, but be in the negative.

Instrument Landing System:

The Instrument Landing System enables the pilot of an
aircraft to make a sale approach and landing ona
rullway even under adverse weather and visibility
condition. The system provides azimuth guidance,
elevation guidance and distance from threshiold infor.
mation.

(i) Function of components of ILS :

{a) Localizer :

The Localizer provides lateral guidance or azimuth
guidance and enables the pilot to align the aircraft
with the centre Hne of the Tunway. Localizer coverage
is provided up to a distance of 25 NM within 10° on
either side of exlended centre Hne of the runway anda
distance of 17 NM within 35°.

(b) Glide Path :

The Gide Path provides elevation guidance, due to
which aircraft is able to descend along pre-detcrimined

66

(c)

()

(i)

(a)

path during landing. The normal glide angle is 2° to
4°. lt provides coverage upto 10 NM within a sector of
8* in the direction of approach.

Outer & Middle Marker :

The Outer and Middle Markers, located at specified
distatice from (he runway along with the approach
path, provide information to the pilot regarding post-
tion of the aircraft with reference to runway threshold.

Monitors :

These componcuts of ILS are provided with their asso-
ctaled moniloring remote control and status indication
system. Montloring system keeps under cheek the
performance of the equipment and in case of malfunc-
Uoning, switches over to standby equipment. In case,
standby equipment also does not perform within speci-
fled tolerance the facility is au tomatically shut down.
Remote control and status indicator system facilitates
i switching ON/OFF from a remote location as well as
to know the status of serviceabilily of the facilities.

As per ICAO requirement, rouline flight checks on ILS
should be carried out ouce i 90+ 15 days.

Localizer is installed al the end of runway 04 at

cnergy supplied by baltery on trickle charge. In case
of power supply fatlure, standby generators supply
power. If generator fails, the batteries comlinue to feed
transnutter tor approx. four hours. The transmilters
are installed in alr-condittioned room located in opera-
onal area at the airport. One fransmilter works at a
Hime and other is slandby. The signuats from equip-

stop end of runway 04,

The transmitted beam covers 35° on elther side of

Distance Measuring Equipment (DME) :

One of the most debated issucs before the Court of Inquiry is
in regard to the non-availability of Distance Measuring Equipmeut
(DME) at Imphal Airport. Whereas the operator along with the
ICPA submitted that in the event of there being a DME, this par-
licular accident would not have taken place as the equipment is
the only instrument from which the pilot is in a position to know
his distance from a particular fix at any airport.

Admittedly and there is no manner of doubt that DME is a
very useful navifational aid and is of very preal assistance lo the
pilot in measuring his distance from VOR or ILS as the case may
be. But the issue arises whether non-availability of DME can be
ascribed to be the cause of ihe aceident or even contributed to the
accident. Before recording my own finding in regard thereto the
evidence tendered before the Court ought to be considered.

72

Mr. S.K. Rastogi, Senlor Acrodrome Officer, Incharge Imphal
Airport, has stated that a DME is useful both for the pilots as well
as for the Control Tower for the purpose of finding distance from
the aircraft to the runway. Incidentally it is to be noted that all
Boeing aircraft are fitted with a DME to correspond with a match-
ing ground cquipinent. Mr. Rastogi, however, stated hat in the
abscuce of a DME there ought not lo be any difficulty in the matter
of measurement of a distance though by an indirect method. Mr.
Rastogi explained the method being based on the ground speed
aud the distance from stations ‘A’ to "Lin colmparison with time.
Though however, he has clearly admitted that this measurement
would be by estimation only and which niay differ by one or two
nautical miles at best, it is the definite statement of Mr. Rastogi
that apart from this estimation the pilot is getling certain other
navigational aids enroute like VOR from where he can take his
position and be more accurate as to how much time he should
take therefrom. It is on evidence that by comparing the previous
reporting position and ihe time one can casily make out the dis-
tance he shall have to cover. In general Mr. Rastogi's evidence is to
the effect that there ought not to be any difficully in the matter of
safe arrival of the aircraft at a particular airfield even without
having a Distance Measuring Equipment though it is very much
desirable to have such an equipment at the station since it can
give the distance without any further calculation. Mr. Rastogi
further stated (hal in the event of disorientation of the pilot, the
DME would be of very great assistance.

Capt. Mehta when asked specifically aboul the importance
and usefulness of this particular equipment, categorically stated
that DME does not form part of the standard ILS procedure bul if
it is available it will definitely help. According to him DME can be
termed to be an additional assistance to the pilot. Capt. Mehta
agrecd with the view that there are olfher ground aids available at
the lmphal airport and the pilot can very easily navigate the air-
crall provided of course he follows aud adheres to the laid down

73


procedures. Capt. A. Raliut also has stated that DME is a helpful
guide.

Mr. Satendra Singh has also stated that DME would be an
extremely helpful guide though cannot be termed to be a very

essential requirement.

The evidence so far disclosed before the Court, therefore,
leaves no manner of doubt that the DME Js a helpful navigational
aid, but cannot be termed to be an essential navigational aid since
in the event of there being no DME, the pilot can co-relate his
distance from the earlier reporting point as also from overhead
VOR by calculation on timings. Incidentally, it is to be noted that
the pilot has been given the let down chart of ILS on the basis of
Umings and speed and not on the basis of a distance. It is a lime-
oriented chart and not a distance-orlented one. The factum of
Ume-orientation is fully within the knowledge of the pilots who fly
into Imphal. It is to be noted further (hat there are approximately
on al average two flighis a day to Imphal and the pilots have been

operaling Uncre without DME for the last several years.

This observation of mine ought not to be interpreted as
undermining the importance and the assistance rendered by DME.
I wish to clarify that abscuce of DME cannot be termed to be the
cause of the accident since even without DME safe landing is
otherwise possible with the assistance of other navigational and
landing aids at Imphal Airport.

Let us ow consider the converse situation also: this parucu-
lar pilot Capt. Sekhar Halder immediately after crossing overhead
Comilla has been giving wrong call-outs both in regard to alli-
tude and distance as more fully appear from the CVR/FDR co-
relation note above. The front panel instrument would definitely
give an indication as to whether he was overhead VOR or not. The
pilot unfortunately did not even bother to proceed on the basis of
the panel instruments - had he followed the same, question of this

74

inquiry would not have arisen. There is not only non-adherence to
the ILS let down chart but a total disregard of the frout panet
instruments - where Is the guarantee, though may be a presump-
tion, that the pilot would have adhered to the DME even if that was
available. I have no hesitation to record thal the entire course of
conduct of the pilot after the aircraft has left overhead Comilla is
rather slip shod and a total disregard for all normal procedures
which a pilot is supposed to follow. It is to be further recorded that
the CVR transcript does not show any emergency of any nature
being experienced by the pilots at the cockpit. The attitude is as
appears from the CVR transcript one of total cool and as stated by
Capt. M.V.V. Rao as a copy-book transmission which when co-
related with FDR depicts otherwise. As a maller of fact Capt. Rao
lumself has stated that he would not have given him the conunand
licence if this FDR was inade available to him after his coumand
check. Iam indeed sorry to record (he same but tulortunately |
am rather helpless considering the facts of the matter under inves-
tigalion. Obviously, a sense of complacency has developed which
has prompted the pilot to proceed in a manner as he has done
while operating the flight from Calcutta to Imphal on the fateful
day.

The other aspect of the matter ought not also to be lost sight
of since a Distance Measuring Equipment was received adnuttedly
at Imphal in July, 1991. In the normal course of events, one
would expect that an equipment costing about rupees one crore
would be immediately comunissioned but this particular equipment
has been allowed to remain at the airport in an unpacked condi-
tion al least till after the accident - a situation which cannot by
any stretch be said to be warranted.

Automatic Direction Finder (ADF) - AD 200 :

During the course of hearing, both Capt. Bagchi appearing
for the Indian Air Passengers Association and Capt. Mulherkar
appearing for Indian Commercial Pilots’ Association, in no uncer-
lain terms suggested that if AD 200 was available wiih (he lmphat

75


Air Traffic Control, the Controller would have known the direction

of the aircraft and thereby could have saved the accident,

AD-200 is a VDF equipment and {{s usefulness cannot in
any way be decried, but with the advancement of technological and
scientific instrumentation of navigational aids, the absence of AD-
200s is not felt in the same way as it used to be about a decade
ago. The installation of VOR at the Indian airports has done away
with the use of the AD-200s in this country. Admittedly, flying in
these days has become totally pilot-oriented. AD-200 would not in
any way assist the pilot on board the aircraft but it is a ground
equipment through which the Controller can identify the direction
of the aircraft and considering therefore the recent pilot-orlented
approach, the National Airports Authority thought it fit to replace
the AD-200s by installation of VORs.

[t is placed on record that I had the painful duty of investi-
galing another air accident involving an F-27 aircraft (VT-DMC)
belonging to Vayudoot on 19th October, 1988 near Guwahali,
Assai. In course of that investigalion also the issue was raised
and when asked the then Coordinating Director Mr. Tekchandani
submitted a brief nole which was quoted al page 80 of the Report.
For convenience sake the same is also quoted hereinbelow :-

"The AD-200 model of VDF equipment was available at
many aerodromes in this country till around
1978/1980. The equipment in use had been serving
at these airfields for over three decades and it had
become increasingly difficult to maintain these equip-
ments in serviceable condition because of the ageing of
the equipment and non-availability of spare parts due
to obsolescence. After carefw consideration of various
aspects Including maintainability of the equipment
upto prescribed standards, their necessity vis-a-vis the
availability of more advanced Nav aids Uke VOR, the
then DGCA decided to withdraw these equipment

radually from service. AD-200C, a later version of

D-200 was ithe last to be withdrawn from service in
this Region at Bhubaneswar some time in 1988 when
i( was no longer possible to maintain the facility to
desired standards.

76

On the queslion of its replacement by latest version, I
would like to submit thal the sanie was not considered
necessary because the VOR, considered an advanced
pilol interpreted and more versatile aid has been
provided at most of the airporis and the NAA is in the
process of providing more VOR coverage of the Indian
alr space. Obvious advantage of tie VOR over the AD-
200 as a direction {lnder Hes in the fact that the pilot
can have its bearing with reference to the VOR tuned
by him from the appropriate instrument in the cockpit
coutinuously, whereas itt the case of AD-200 the bear-
ing of the aircraft derived alt the ground had to be
comununicated by the Air Traffic Control to the aircraft
whenever desired or necessary. The only advantage of
AD-200 over VOR, if it can be so termed, lies in the
fact that the ATC would know the direction of the
aircraft in the case of AD-200 and not in the case of
VOR which is a pilot interpreted aid.

I may also add that the latest generation of VDF
equipment of the type in use at four international
airports (imported from USA manufactured by Servo
Corporation) ts not indigenously available and that it is
a costly equipment. :

Cousidering the huge cost involved in providing the
VDF equipment at all (he airports in the country par-
ticularly in hard foreign currency, {| would be apprect-
ated by His Lordship and the Learned Assessors that
NAA can go for this only at the cost of ils present plans
to improve and modernise the communication and
Navigational aids in more crucial areas."

The findings on this particular issue so far as the earlter
investigalion is concerned ought also to be noted. At page 81 of
the earlier reporl my observations to the following cffect were
recorded :-

"While it is true thal AD-200 is an out-dated machin-
ery, but its importance and usefulness cannot be
decried. But considering the improved phenomenon of
the ground aids, in my view, it would not be fair to put
any blame on to the National Airports Authority as was
sought to have been done by various parties appearing
before the Court of Inquiry. With the AD-200, the
ground control is in a position to know the location of
the aircraft and in the earlicr days used to communi-
cate the same to the aireraft, but with the introduction
of VOR, the same being now considered to be a pilot
interpreted instrument and a more effective aid so that
the pilot-in-command himself is ina position to locate
his locations rather than depending upon the ground

77


control to inform him. In that view of the matter,
installation of further AD-200s on the threshold of the
21st century, in my view, does not warrant further
consideration aud Faceept the submissions made by
Mr. Tekchandani ou behalf of the National Airports
Authority."

In the present context also since there is no new material on
record or any additional evidence being adduced before the Court
justifying a different view, I do not intend to retract from the earlier
observations, but reiterate the same and note that with the ad-
vancement of technology question of introduction of AD-200 al this
Stage docs not arise. It is nol a replacement for a radar though a
definite land aid which has lost its importance in the present day

context.
Locator Beacons :

As narrated above the Instrument Landing System caters for
the existence of an outer marker signal - it may be through a fan
marker and a locator beacon or only a fan marker. Whereas the
fan marker has a limited vertical range with a cone over which it
provides audio and visual indications, the locator beacon Is a non-
directional beacon having a much wider range and would thus be a
lore positive assistance to the pilot in the matter of locating his
position vis-a-vis the outer marker. The locator beacon will indi-
cate as to the direction of the outer marker so as to allow the pilot
lo take correclive measures if there be any. Locator beacon is an
addiUonal assistance but cannot as such be termed to be an
essential navigational equipment in an ILS procedure. Needless to
say, however, that existeuce of a fan marker will suffice in so far as
the safety aspect is concerned, more so by reason of the fact that
the pilot is supposed to follow the procedure in ils observance
ralher than in its deviations. At the lnphal Airport, there is a fan
marker which gives both audio and the light blip and that by itself,
in iy view, would be a sufficient protecHon lo (he pilot for a sale
landing. As noted above, locator beacon is an addilional assist-
ance and il is always better to have a further assistance if that is

78

1 be

available. Locator beacon is nothing but a low powered NDB
through which the pilots are ina position to locate the outer
inarker with exactitude. tt cannot thus, (therefore, be regarded as a
mandatory essential navigational ald but an additional assistance.
It is on record that on the fateful day the fan markers at the outer
marker and the middle marker locations duly emitted both audio
and visual signals even just before the unfortunate crash as re-
ported by Capt. L.P. Mishra being the Pilot-in-Commanid of fight
IC-889 on 16th August, 1991. For convenience sake relevant

This Court, however, is not in a position to appreciate the
role of the Naional Airports Authority in the matter of Search and
Rescue. Mr. Rastogi, Senior Acrodronic Officer, and an official of
National Airports Authorily fn no uncertain terms has stated (hat
in the event of there being an air crash in the vicinity of the airport
only ihe National Airports Authority is expected to take steps for
search and rescue but not otherwise since that would be the
responsibility of the State Government. I am to record here my
tolal disagreement with this practice of the National Airports
Authority. The National Airports Authority is one of the principle
wings of the entire Civil Aviation industry in this country and it is
truce that their functions are specified but thal, however, does not

84


mican and imply that there shall not be any obligation on their part
to be a party in the matter of search aud rescue in an air crash,
even Hf the crash takes place ata place which caunot be said to be
within the vicinily of Une airporl. Mr. Rastogi has been frank
cnough to state before the Court that on the first information of the
crash near Loktak Lake in the afternoon of 16th August, 1991, he
went along with the police officials but when nothing was found in
and around the area of Loktak Lake, he came back without any
further aitempt to locate the wreckage. He is quite content in
fiiforming the Deputy Commissioner of Police to look for the air-
craft. I did not find in him even at the witness box any anxiety in
locating lhe crash site. As a matter of fact, according to his own
statement, he got the information about the crash sile next day
morning when all the newspapers in the ceuntry reported the
accident including ils location. Is il expected of a Civil Aviation
industry man to know of the crash site on the next morning, ty my
view, the answer cannot be in the affirmative. Safety of the people
ought to be the foremost consileration for a Civil Aviation industry
man in the country. Assuming there were some survivors, what
would have happened to them if no medical attention was made
available within shortest possible tine - one does not know. Mr.
Rastogi is the man on-the-spot, if he takes this attitude and slicks
to his duty chart, it would be deemed to be a very sad day for the
Civil Aviation industry in the country. The obligation to render
assistance under any circumstances should be a prime considera-
tion to an Acrodrome Officer. As a matter of fact, Mr. Rastogi’s
entire conduct does uot seem to be in consonance will a sense of
public safely and when the aircraft is (o land, he is quile content to
be “in his chamber and talking to the clients.” Thal is the attitude
which has been exhibited even in Court and which by no stretch
according to me is proper. Lam conscious of the fact that the
Seidor Acrodrome Officer has had some administralive work to do
but that does not, however, mean and imply thal when there were
oily Uiree fighis and all restricted by about mid afternoon, lhe
Senior Acrodrome Officer will be in his Chamber talking to his
clients at the time of the flight. There must be an anxiety in the

85


mind of the Senior Acrodrome Officer as to how things are proceed-
ingg at the Control Tower - there ought to be a scnuse of belouging: to
an Olficer and he must be sincere for the purpose for which he las
been asked to be there to cater for safety of the aircrall including
the passengers. Docs the conduct of the Senior Acrodrome Officer
show such an anxiety or sincerity - 1 am afraid the answer cannot
but be in the negative. It is true that Mr. Rastogi is a trained and
experienced Officer, but the country has not been able to obtain
the benefit of his training or his experience - a state of affairs
which cannot by any stretch be said to be very encouraging. It is
for the National Airports Authority to inculcate a sense of belong-
ing on to its Officers so that public safety in not in any way im-
paired or there is any compromise in regard thereto.

Mercly by sending an intimation to the police authorities, i
iy view, will not do - further steps are required to be taken which
Mr. Rastogi, according to me, has failed.

ll. WRECKAGE INFORMATION :

Details of wreckage information would appear from the
report of the Inspector of Accidents and for convenience sake, the
saine Is quoled hereinbelow :-

"This aircraft impacted on the hill at a point approxi-
matcly 200 feet from the top of Thangjing hill which ts
located 20.5 NM South-West of Iniphal Acrodrome with
a bearing of 220° from VOR. Although the aircraft had
broken to pieces, wreckage was limiled to a small area
(400M X LOOM) and could be viewed from the top of
the hill. The inclination of the slope at the point of
impact is about 60° to 70°. The major parts of the
airplane were strewn with the empennage of the air-
craft at the lower most point of the crash sile. Onc
wing was found to the left and another similar struc-
ture to the right. These were almost half-way of the
crash site (longitudinally). Just above the right hand
side wing, was one undercarriage identified as LH
undercarriage. The nose wheel was almost on the top
of the crash site. Due to massive destruction and
parts disturbed by the local inhabitants, it was not
possible to co-relate many positions during this
wreckage examination. Alse, there was post-accident
fire which destroyed parts.

However, it could be determined that the landing gear
was In UP position as found from the landing gear
operating actuator. Fore, mid and aft flaps were seen
along with right hand wing separated from the fuse-
lage. The flaps were out which corresponds to ap-
proach position. The horizontal stabilizer was cut off
about 30" from extreme outboard end and right hand
horizontal stabilizer was in one piece. Rear pressure
bulkhead was separated from the fuselage. The fin,
which separated out was clearly visible with L.A.
monogram while engine was traced on the L.H. side,
another cugine casing was lying on the L.IH. side of
crash site."

Mr. D.K. Roy, the Deputy Engineering Manager of Indian
Alrlines at Calcutta, being one of the persons who also reached the
crash site has stated thal there was practically no road and it was
the very Lough journey. Mr. Roy stated that while he was going up,
he found quite a lot of people conung down from the crash site with
sinall articles belonging to the aircraft. At the crash site, the CVR
was found out by one of the technicians of Indian Airlines being

91 .


carried by one person on his back. There was also a photographer
and the latter was asked to fake the photograph of the man carry-
dug the CVR. The matter was reported thereatter to the police and
the man was (raced out and the CVR was recovered wilhout there
being any damage and with proper seals. The FDR also was locat-
ed somewhere else around 30 kms away from the crash point in a
village and was hidden in a banana bush. This FDR would not
have been recovered but by reason of a generous taxi driver who
came and reported the factum of the removal of the FDR from the
crash site to a village and hiding it under a banana bush by a
villager. It is to be placed on record that the FDR was recovered
without also there being any further damage to the recorder ex-
cepting the impact damage. A wreckage distribution chart for
convenience sake, is also annexed hereto and marked with letter

12. GENERAL OUTLINE OF INVESTIGATION BY THE COURT
OF INQUIRY :

Government of India vide Notification No.AV.15013/5/91-
SSV dated 26th September, 1991 constituted the Court of lnquiry
under Rule 75 of the Aircratt Rules, 1937, wherein | was appointed
for the formal investigalion of the crash of the Indiai Airlines flight
being No. IC-257 (the route being Caleutla-lmphal-Dimapur) on
16th August, 1991, near Thangjing Hill at Lmphal, Manipur, to be
assisted by Mr. B.P. Baliga, a former Director of Engineering, Air
India and Wing Cdr. P. Ashoka, Executive Direclor, Hindustan
Aeronautics Limited, as Assessors. Mr. A.K. Sinha, Senior Air
Safety Officer, in the Directorate of Director General of Civil Avia-
tion, Calcutta, was appointed as the Secretary to the Court of
Inquiry. The Court was requested to submit its report by 31st
December, 1991, but subsequently, however, the {ime was extend-
ed ull 30th April, 1992.

It is to be noted that Mr. R.K. Paul, Regional Controller of Air
Safety in the Directorate of Director General of Civil Aviation,
Calcutia, was appointed as the Inspector of Accidents by the Direc-
tor General of Civil Aviation immediately after the unfortunate
crash on 16th August, 1991.

On the constilution of the Court, Mr. Paul did call on me and
apprised me of the work which has already been done by him
including examination of some witnesses. | requested Mr. Paul to
continue and complete his investigation in the matter with utmost
expedition.

On 8th October, 1991, I along with the Assessors named
above, together with the Director Gencral of Civil Aviation Mr. MLR.
Sivaraman, Deputy Director General of Civil Aviation Mr. H.S.
Khola, Director Air Safety in the Directorate of the Director General
of Civil Aviation Headquarters Mr. Satendra Singh, the Inspector of
Accident Mr. R.K. Paul and the Secretary to the Court of Inquiry
Mr. A.K. Sinha reached Imphal for the purpose of an on-the-spot

93

survey of the crash sile and obtaining necessary details in regard
to the crash of the alreraft (VT-EFL). The Indian Air Foree authort-
ics did provide a helicopter for the purpose of a survey of the
crash site, but in spite of our best efforts by hovering over the area
in question in the helicopter, the survey was not possible by reason
of the inclement weather conditions and the Thangjing Hill being
overcast with clouds.

I, however, did have detailed discusstons with the State
Police and the acrodrome officials as also the doctors who per-
formed the post mortem exanunations of the bodies of the passen-
gers and that of the crew members of the ill-fated aircraft. Next
day morning also there was an attempt to have an on-the-spot
survey bul continuance of inclement weather prevented us from
being at the crash site.

On return to Calcutta, however, I along with the officials of
the DGCA and my Assessors inspecied the engineering division al
the hangars of Indian Airlines at Calcutta and ascertained the
niode and method of doing the overnight checks. The Chief Engi-
neering Manager Mr. S. R. Chakravarty and the Deputy Engineer-
