also present at the discussions. There were further discussions
with the Executive Director, Calcutta, NAA Mr. S.C. Goswami and

On 11th October, 1991, | proceeded to Deli for an assess-

Headquarters. The two assessors were also present at the time of

Some confusion arose as the exact location of the crash site
during the hearing by reason of which I directed the Deputy Direc-
tor General of Civil Aviation to arrange for a further visit to Imphal
for an on-the-spot Inquiry. To facilitate the issue necessary ar-
rangements werc made to position an Emergency Locator Trans-
milter (ELT) at the crash site so that while flying on an A-320
aircraft necessary signals may be obtained in order to deterniine
the correct distance of the crash site from the VOR point. Indian
Space Research Organisation, Bangalore, was also requested to
pick up the signal through satellite and indicate the coordinates of
the source of signal. Though the ELT was placed at the crash sile,
but waforlunately due to bad weather conditions as also hoi-avail-
ability of the VOR at Imphal airport the flight to lunphal via
Guwahati could not be operated on that day. The Bangalore Space
Research Centre, however, obtained the signals and subinitted the
approximate coordinates of the crash site, but in order to confirm
the exact location, the Surveyor General of India was requested to
provide this Court with necessary coordinates which was made lo
this Court by way of a certification as slated above, suffice however
to record here that the distance between the VOR tmphal and the
top of the Thangjing hill has been certified to be 20.19 NM.

At the hearing, the issue of clearance of the hill on pilots
immediate reaction, upon the sounding of the GPWS came up for
consideration and in order to appreciate it better, | went to Hyd-

13. ANALYSIS OF EVIDENCE TENDERED BEFORE THE
COURT OF INQUIRY:

The evidence tendered before the Court of Inquiry for the
purpose of correct appreciation may be sub-divided under the
following heads :-

i) Possibility of sabotage;

ii) Airworthiness of the aircraft;

iil) Calearance to direct outbound approach;

iv) Flight path followed by the ill-fated aircraft vis-a-vis
the operational fight plan and the ILS let down chart;

v) Role of co-pilot during the flight.

vi) Possibility of wrong indication by VOR;

vil) GPWS warning and crew response.

It will be convenient to deal with the above seriatin:

i) Possibility of Sabotage:

The Indiau Airlines Bocing-737 (VT-EFL) while on a sched-
uled flight on route Calcutta-luphal-Dimapur being flight No.IC-
257 crashed near Imphal Airport into the hill slopes of Thangjing
Hill with its nose hitting a place approximately 300 fect below the
summit of the hill. Major C.B. Lal, Controller of Explosives,
Bureau of Civil Aviation Security, Calculta Airport, visited the
crash site for an on-the-spot visual inspecUion and examination of
the wreckage on 17th and 18th Atigust, 1991 for the purpose of
ascertainment of the factum of there being any sabolage. Itis the
definite evidence of Major Lal thal he has not seen any positive
signature of explosives or explosion in the aircraft on thle wreckage
examination. Major Lal pointed out (hat if there was a bomb
planted on the aircraft and an explosion resulting Uherefrom, there
would have been definite signatures on the metal as well as in the
various wreckages of the aircraft and on the dead bodies. In his
evidence he has explained the posilive signatures as (a) the forma-
Hon of distinctive surface effects such as piling or very sinall

100

craters formed in metal surface caused by extremely high velocity
input from small particles of explosives and such craters would
have ratsed or have rolled over edges and often have explosive
residue in the bottom of the craters; (b) the surface cliecis would
have shown metal fragments produced by explosives and would
have caused deformation mechanism which are peculiar (o high
rate of strain at normal temperature together with a dislocation
movement: because the Process in an explosion is thermally acti-
vated ata very high rate of strain. Major Lal explained that in
some metals such as copper, iron and steel deformation in the
crystal of the metals takes place by twinning, that is to Say, the
parallel lines or cracks cutting across the crystal. He went on to
say (hat such Phenomenon can ouly occur if the Spectinen or the
subject has been subjected to extreme shock wave leading at veloc-
ity in the order of 8,000 metres per second.

On a pointed question from the Court as to whether the
wreckage, as it is in the mnalter under investigation, can have the
Same affectation in the eveul of an object hilting the aircraft from
ground, to wit, a missile, the answer of Major Lal, however, was in
the negative, since it will create a perforation and then as usual
the explosive blast would be there. Major Lal observed that in that
event, there are also chances of pieces of nussile overhead portion
being available at the wreckage site and as such air external object
being thrown and lutting the aircraft cannot but be ruled out and
so Js the sabotage by micaus of a bomb being planted on board the
aircraft. The report of Major Lal depicts that on exalnination of
wreckage, centre of explosion and explosion pressure profile could
nol be determined as damage to the aircrait's one Wilf, nose,
cockpit and passenger cabin was total and slinilar as in the case of
resultant impact of high speed aircraft. The Phenomenon of
fragmentation, curling/riuglet effects and spike looth fractures in
metal surface were also not observed on visual examination. The
phenomenon of cupping/dishing of metal Stuface, high velocity
penetration of fragments in interior furniture, cushion and plastic
fitments due to explosion were not observed. Major Lal reported

101

fo

that in the light of visual Inspection of the crash Site and examina-
fion of wreckage in-situ, the theory of mid-air bomb exploston and
sabotage is rejecled. For convenience sake, however, (he report of
Major Lal on the issuc of Sabotage is annexed hereto and Marked
with letter ‘J' (ihe photographs and charts though cuclosed with

Ji) Airworthiness of the Aircraft -
—eeemettess of the Aircraft

As noled above under the head Alrcraift Information, this

and inspections sUpulated by the Directorate General of Civil Avia-

from Delhi to Calcutta via Lucknow, Patiig and Rauchi and then if

when the aircraft was in flight cither from Delhi to Calcutta as

While it is true aud as appears from the evidence that (here

From the available records, il appears that this particular

Needless to record here however, that during the (light no

While it is true that the aircraft was otherwise safe and

Another redeeming feature came lo light during the course of

The transmissions above, depict that when the aircraft

Two very senior and experienced pilots of the operator were

cannot possibly be any scope for avoiding overhead VOR while

conversation corroborates such statements.

Capt. Mehta in answer to Question No.215 has stated that
the pilol has to go overhead aud by direct outbound what he meant
was, he shall not join the holding pattern and turn right by 30° so
that he can pick up 217° and proceed. Further in auswer to Ques-

for direct outbound, it is just on conung overhead and proceeding

is to be noted that on a poinied question from the Court, Capt.
Mehta stated that wtih 8 octa cloud he would never ask for a
pernussion for a direct outbound without Joining the holding pat-

Capt. Rahut, another very expericuced pilot of the operator
also in no uncertalu terms stated that even if one wants to come
direct outbound, there is no escape but to come overhead VOR and
then go on radial 217 and direct outbound does not mean that he
will not go overhead VOR (vide Q. 130 to 134).

107


On a plain reading of the statements, il is, therefore, clear
and apparent Ghat the permission to set course directly outbound
obviously mean and tiply, when overlicad and not lo set coarse
directly outbound for ict down from that point itself. Both Capt.
Mehta and Capt. Rahut have in no uncertain ferms stated that
pilot shall have to come overhead VOR in any event. The ATCO
also if under that belief, cannot thus be said to be wrong more so
by reason of the fact that a distance of 12 miles cannot be covered
in just two seconds. It is an inconceivable idea that a pilot would
comience a let down without getting a ground fix namely, over-
head VOR. Further, it 1s to be noted that the pilot, in fact, at
07:11:26 UTC transmitted on an enquiry from the Tower about the
position of the atrerafl as "We are approaching overhead". Tis tn
evidence thal selling course directly outbotnd can be affected by
making a loop or a tear drop entry and it is as per the choice of the
Pilot-in-Command. The Senior Acrodrome Officer Mr. Rastogi has
staled such a tear drop entry is permissible and as a matter of fact
the pilot among themselves have decided to make a tear drop entry
overhead VOR. Incidentally it is to be noted that al the approach
briefing the pilot has in no uncertalu terms stated that visual or
otherwise he will come overhead VOR at 10,000 [cet and obtain
radial 217 after a tear drop entry.

Can it thus be stated that the transmission from the Tower
to the pilot in approval of the request of the pilot to go direct
outbound has really mislead the pilo€ - tn mry view the auswer
cannot but be in the negative. The pilot is responsible for safe
landing at the airport - he has to decide what approach he will
make; Une AFCO does not have any say in the matter, though
however, some amount of extra carc on the part of the ATCO would
undoubtedly have been betler. It is the standard procedure that
the pilot shall have to report overhead VOR, as a matter of fact the
ATCO did ask the pilot to report when overhead VOR and the pilot
in turn has acknowledged it by using the expression ‘Roger’.
Subsequent transmission approaching overhead from the pilot and
“understand proceeding on a radial 217" ought also to be consid-

108


ered. Radial 217 ts ihe correct flight path which a pilot should
follow - reminding the pilot of lis radial even if the pilot is proceed-
Ing direct outbound - fu my view ought to have given the pilot
sufficient indication so far as safety is concerned from the ATCO's
point of view. It is for the pilot to manoeuvre the aircraft and
obtain (he radial 217 and not for the ATCO to bring him on to
radial 217 from overhead VOR - aftcrall the pilot is incharge of the
aircraft and not the ATCO. Considering the matter from the other
point of view, even one cannot but come lo an inescapable conclu-
sion that the pilot understood by such transmission from the
Tower that he shall have to come overhead VOR as otherwise he
would not have transmitted at a subsequent point of time that he
is approaching overhead VOR. This factum of subsequent trans-
mission negates the theory of the pilot being-mislead by the grant
of permission for direct outbound. It is needless to say that itis a
pilot who has sought permission to go direct outbound and not the
ATCO on his own required the aircraft to go direct outbound. The
ATCO only approved the approach of the pilot which under the
normal circumstances the ATCO permits. Assuming, the pilot
asks for a visual approach since the runway is in sight - would the
ATCO negative such an approach - in the normal circumstances
the answer is in the negative - the reason being the pilot ought to
know what approach to make for safe landing of the aircraft and
this is a standard procedure to grant permission to a visual ap-
proach for landing when such a permission is sought for. The
transmission under consideration is also one such approval on the
piloUs secking such a permission - moreso, by reason of the fact
that the approach Js a pilot-orlented approach and not a radar-
vectored approach whereii the ATCO is the persou responsible for

sale vectoring of the aircraft upto final approach for landing.

Turning now on to the other aspect of the matter as to
whether this particular call-out can be termed to be a justifiable
call-out - Capt. Mehta in no uncertain terms stated il to be an
absolutely wrong call-out. Let us now try to analyse the siluation
in a slightly more greater detail. The flight plan available to the

109


pilot depicts that he should approach overhead VOR with 070°
heading which mean and fmply that the afrerafl should be ap
proaching the VOR trom the South-West direction. Havings the
heading in mind, it would be a near linpossibility to obtain out-
bound radial 217 before outer marker unless, however, the pilot
comes overhead VOR and follows a tear drop turn or joins the VOR
hold for proceeding outbound since the angle of turn would other-
wise be extremely difficult to manoeuvre.

On the basis of the above, it can therefore be safely conclud-
ed that this particular call-out secking permission to go direct
outbound cannot be termed to be a proper call-out but a wrong
call-out as Capt, Mehta deseribed it. As a matter of fact, the pilot
wanted to cut short his timings, which has unfortunately placed
him much beyond the safety zone. The details of the pilot's action,

however, would be dealt with hereinbelow.

iv) Flight Path followed by the ill-fated aircraft vis-a-vis the
Flight Plan and ILS Let Down Chart :

The operator for all its routes evolved various operational
fight plans detailing out the headings at different points of time for
the entire route and Imphal is no exceplion. The flight plan for
Calcutta-lmphal sector categorically states the MORA - 10,000
feet. In ordinary common English parlance MORA - 10,000 feet
mean and imply safe altitude upto 10,000 feet. The clevation of
the airport has also been given at 2,536 feet. The details of Hight
plan for Calculta-Imphal sector would appear from page 5 of this
report; Chapter 2 "The Unfortunate Flught". The flight plan depicts
a total flight Ume from Calcutta to overhead VOR Imphal to be 52
nunutes and a further cight minutes (ime has been added to facill-
tate landing at the airport on ILS let down chart from overhead
VOR. Considering the {ght plan as above, the entire flight timings
from Calcutta to Imphal is to the extent of GO minutes. The flight
plan takes care upto overhead VOR Imphal and the let down chart
thereafter guides the pilot to land Saiely un the airport upon follow-


ing the standard ILS procedure as per the let down chart and thal
precisely is the reason why the let down chart conimenees from
overhead VOR at lmphal Airport. There is definite evidence that in
the event the pilot follows the flight plan and the let down chart,
question of there being any hazard or there betng any compromise
with safety would not arise.

The FDR data as available from the FDR read-out, indicates
that the Pilot-in-Command of the ill-fated aircraft IC-257, in fact,
followed the flight plan in its entirely upto Comilla and there
exists no deviation whatsoever froin the flight plan. The devia-
ons, however, started only after the aircraft crossed over Comil-
la. FDR data records that instead of 007 heading, the aircraft
followed 053, completely by-passing overhead Agartala. The CVR
transcript, however, records a transmission at 06:51:44 UTC as
approaching overhead Alpha Alpha Tango, bul as noted above, the
FDR data tell a different story. The aircraft was nowhere near
Alpha Alpha Tango - why this call-out then - obviously to give an
finpression thal the light plan is being obcyed in its enlirely and
there is no departure therefrom. If the pilot does not give such a
call-out, the same obviously would be noted and he might be put
to books which no pilot desires. Capt. Mchta describes it as a total
indiscipline of the pilot and so does Capt. M.V.V, Rao. It is neither
fair nor reasonable on the part of the pilot of an aircraft to give a
wrong call-out. This is not the end of the Slory but just the begin-
ning. Question obviously crops up as to how a pilot having fol-
lowed the chart in totality upto a certain polnt has deviated there-
afler - the answer is not very far to seek: From Calcutta to Conmnil-
la there is every likelihood of the fight path being monitored by the
Radar Controller both at Calcutta and at Dhaka bul beyond that
question of there being any screcning of the flight path would not
arise since there is no radar control at Agartala or at Khumbigram
or at Imphal. The pilot, therefore, innovates his own procedure
and a new flight plan cutting corners here and there. | hereby
record my concurrence with the observations of Capt. Mehta that
there is total Indiscipline - why on earth a Pilot would report

lil

overhead Agartala whien, in fact, he is nowhere tear VOR Agartala
- a grossest possible indiscipline. The FDR data shows that in-
slead of having a heading of 007 the pllot-in-conunand in faet
proceedcd toa heading 053 and continued to do so for a period of
about 12 minutes, when in fact, he was Supposcd to fly with a
heading of 007 for a period of four minutes and thereafter with a
heading of 070 till such time he comes overhead hnphal. It is to be
noted that after flying about 12 nunutles, the pilot changed his
heading to around 81° and continue to fly on the saine heading for
a period of about nine minutes and subsequently changed his
heading close to 70°. But why this departure from the flight plan -
cutling corners with an aircraft is not only unsafe but connotes a
lotal lack of professional e(hies for an airman. ‘wo experienced
pilots and the senior trainer of the operator have deprecated such
an attiude of the pilot and ascribed it to be an act of indiscipline,
Culling corners is not the only Jndiscipline noted in {he pilot. As
noted above, wrong call-outs is another redeeming feature of this
particular pilot operating the ill-fated flight. ATCO's enquiry as to
the altitude has also been Une victim of such an indiscipline. The
FDR/CVR co-relation note as above, dealt with this aspect of the
matter in detail, suffice it however to record that this attitude of
transmitting wrong altitudes to the ATCO may Cven result to a
much worst catastrophe had this been happening in a major inter-
national airport where air traffic density is more.

Incidentally Wis to be noled Chat tight Plan ought to be
observed in its observance rather (han in its departure (herefrom,
Pam constrained, however, to record that there was an eltort on
the part of the operator to give an liupression to the Court that one
can deviate from the flight plan and no exception can be taken in
regard thereto. Though, however, subsequently on pointed ques-
uions from the Court, the evidence tend to show that it is a ques-
tion of ‘must’ as regards the observance of the flight plan and it
can only be varied or be deviated from Upon special permission
from the Air Traffic Controller and that also is more or less re-
Stricled to unusual Situations and Ju the event of visual landing -

which is understandable. A pilot expericncing heavy turbulence
and a cb cloud, then with the permission of the Air Traffic Control-
ler, he might deviate his course bul come back to the required
track afler avoiding the above eventualities - these are not tn the
nornial expectations but under unusual circumstances and
nobody can take any exception therefor, but in the normal circun-
stances it is now an admitted State of facts that the pilot should
adhere to the fight plan in its obedience.

On the basis of the above noted tabulation, three main
deviations with respect to Might plan ceme to light and the same

are being voted hereinbelow for convenience sake :-

As mentioned earler the total fight time from Calcutta to
overhead Imphal is 52 minules as per the opcraional [light plan.
The ill-fated aircraft took-off from Calcutla at 06:24 UTC and
hence was estimated to come overhead Lnphal VOR al 0716 UTC.
After the aircraft came in contact with Imphal, it reported at
06:54:22 hours UTC that it was maintaining flight level 290 and
was estimating overhead Imphal at 07:16. After reaching over-
head Comilla, the aircraft instead of going overhead Agartala,
took almost a direct route to Imphal and ilappears that as a result
of Uus short-cut, the flight crew reported to Imphal ATC at
07:04:48 that its revised estimated time of arrival overhead fphal
was 07:12 hours UTC indicating thereby thal it would reach
Imphal four minutes ahead of the earlier esthuated time of arrival.

In accordance with the flight plan, the aircraft is expected to
conumence descent about 45 minutes after take off from Calcutta
which in the present case would be 07:09 hours UTC. From the
co-relation of CVR and FDR data, it is observed that the aircraft
had actually commenced descent at 06:59 hours UTC i.e. 10
minutes prior to the expected time of commencement of descent.
Even if the revised ETA ts considered, the descent should have
commenced around 0705 hours UTC. No valid reason could be
given under the prevailing circumstances as to why the command-
er commenced descent so carly - it only reflects disregard to the
laid down operating procedures by the fight crew. Ho may be
mentioned thal in such situation when the crew commences
descent much prior to the time indicated in the flight plan, the
estiinated time for the flight would vary on account of change of
true air speed and the commander would not be in a position to
estimate his correct lime of arrival at the destinalion. It is likely
that this may have been a factor that the crew were not knowing
exactly their position while approaching Imphal and gave incorrect

position reports. Since on account of early commencement of
descent, true air speed during descent would be lower (han the
true air speed taken into account for estimating Uight time given in
the flight plan, it is Hkely that when the crew estimated that they
would be approaching overhead VOR, they were actually much
away from the VOR which may have been a factor for carly
commencement of right turn for ILS let down. It is, therefore,
needless to emphasise that such deviations from the flight plan
could cause serious problems during the flight. Incidentally, it is
to be noted that Capt. Bagchi representing the Air Passengers
Association also expressed more or less similar view in his argu-
ments. Capt. Bagchi submitted "The flight as per Operalional
Flight Plan should have coumuenced descent from FLL. 290 al O709
or 0705 - revised; which is 1 mt. after abeam Ku. The descent
permission was given at 06:58:51 approx. 6 mits. earlier, thereby
losing TAS and this actually delayed arrival over Iniphal, which
was probably not considered. This miscalculation made the flight
lo turn right for outbound earlier". It is, however, clarified that
descent clearance may be given by the ATCO at an earlicr point of
time but actual cominencement of the descent is dependent upon
the pilot and the ATCO has no say {11 regard thereto. In any event
in the matter under consideration the descent clearance was given
to the aircraft by the ATCO at the request of the flight crew (NOTE
- Instead of Silchar it should be read as Imphal in the CVR tran-
script at 06:58:34).

Another deviation which was observed from the Cockpit
Voice Recorder transcript was that at 07:08:44 hours UTC, the
PHlot-in-Conmand had iuformed the Co-pilot that they were 25
iniles from Imphal at that time, which was perhaps on the basis of
some ground features. At 07:09:45 hours UTC 1.e. about one
minute and one sccond thereafter, the crew reported to ATC
Imphal that they were 12 miles inbound. The above information
tend to indicate as if the aireralt had covered a distance of 13 tiles
in about one minute which is an impossibility. The position report
at 07:09:45 was perhaps given on the basis of revised ETA of 07:12

11?


which got effected on account of early commencement of descent
and which does not seem to have been taken tito account by the

flight crew.

The above deviations in the actual flight path followed by the
aircraft from the latd down operational Night plan reflect scant
respect [or laid down operating procedures by the flight crew and
evidently lead to unsafe situations during flight. It also shows that
the crew did not know exact position of the aircraft during ap-
proach to Imphal primarily on account of deviations from the flight
plan. This is evident from the fact that at 07:11:29 the flight crew
reported that they were approaching overhead Imphal VOR where-
as they were actually at that tne about 15 nautical miles away
from the VOR. Based on their estimated lime of arrival of 07:12,
which was very much at variance with actual position, the (light
crew commicuced right Curn for direct outbound al 07:11:42 when,
in fact, they were still about 14 nautical miles away from the VOR.
At 07:12:13 hours UTC the flight crew reported that they were on
outbound leg for let down and were descending to 5,000 feet. At
that time, ihe aircraft was sUll about 10 nautical miles away from
the VOR. From the above it is abundantly clear that the crew had
no precise idea about their position and coupled with the fact that
they did nol report overhead VOR, they weut far away from the
normal ILS path during the let down.

The actual path followed by the aircraft during ils approach
to Imphal vis-a-vis the ILS let down path may be seen in Annexure
‘K.

In accordance with the ILS let down chart of lmphal Airport,
the aircraft is required to descend from 10,000 {cet to 8.000 feet in
the holding area and then proceed outbound on radial 217 fora
duration of two and half minutes from overhead VOR. The air-
cralt’s speed during the outbound leg is restricted to a maxinium
of 170 knots. In the process of going outbound when the aircraft
flics over middle marker and outer marker, it gets audio and visual


signals in the cockpit. During the outbound leg the aircraft
should descend from 8,000 feet to 5,000 feet. As mendoned carer
the aircraft did not report overhead VOR and, in fact, commenced
right turn when it was about 14 nautical miles away from the VOR
and at an allitude of 10,000 feet. While executing turn, the air-
craft started descent. On completion of the right turn at about
07:13:06 UTC, the aircraft flew in a direction of 196 to 210° for
about t nunute 35 seconds i.e. upto 07:14:41 UTC and during this
period the aircraft descended to an altitude of about 6,880 [eet,
Thereafter, the aircraft turned left in the form of procedure turn.

From the above it is seen that the outbound leg was totally
al variance from the normal outbound leg of an ILS procedure.
The aircraft was nowhere on radial of 217, tt never came overhead
middle marker or outer marker which is apparent from: the ground
path shown in Annexure ‘K’ vis-a-vis the ILS path. This lact is also
borne out from the CVR transcript which shows that there was no
audio signal of middle marker or outer marker recorded on the
tape during the oulbound Jeg. The ill-fated aircraft went on the
outbound leg for about one minute and thirty-five seconds as
against two and a half minutes normally required. The aircralt
speed during the outbound leg was between 175 to 180 knots,
which was higher than the maximum permissible specd of 170
knots. During the outbound leg the aircraft descended to an alti-
tude of 6,880 feet whicreas as per the ILS lel down chart it should
descend to an allitude of 5,000 feet prior to contumencement of
procedure turn.

From the CVR tape transcript it is noled that at 07:14:38
UTC there is an intra-cockpit conversation "Iwo minutes up"
indicating as if the crew were Uming the outbound leg and at that
juncture it had flown for two minutes on the outbound leg though
in fact it had flown only one minute and 35 seconds as noted
above. Admittedly the aircraft did uot come overacad VOR from
which the time calculation would have to be made as per the let
down chart for a period of two and a half minutes. There is no


evidence on record neither any onc of the partics have tried to give
any explanation as to Che fix from which the time ealeulation as
appears [from the CVR transcript was effected. Obviously, the
pilots were proceeding on the basis of some fix aud upon the expiry
of the (wo minutes have recorded the same as noted above. Capt.
Melita has stated that a pilot in the event of malfunctioning of the
VOR instrument would always check through other cockpit panel
instruments and as such the possibility of wrong VOR instrument
indication having misled the pilot does not arise. The only pre-
sumption is that the pilots well knew that they are not following
the or adhering to the route chart and in that event probably with
some ground fix they have started calculating the time. It is,
however, to be noted here thal the let down chart in fact, provides
for a fight of two and a half nunutes on the outbound leg but on
the basis of the FDR read-out there is no Inanner of doubt that the
pilots were taking a turn fmumedately after the iuftra-cockpit con-
versation - two minutes up. This curtailinent of time corroborates
the presumption that the pilol well knew that they are not follow-
ing the procedure in terms of the ILS let down chart and as such
there is no manner of doubt that turning short of VOR on to the
right hand side is a deliberate act on the part of the pilot and not
by reason of any misleading information as otherwise they would
not have even curtailed the two and a half minutes leg to one
minute thirly five seconds ouly and they obviously came to thie
conclusion that this curtailment of nearly a minute leg on the out-
bound would bring him exactly on the same position where he is
supposed to take the procedure turn in terms of the let down
procedure and be able to capture the localiser and glide slope and
other ground aids therealler for landing. This particular allempt,
however, on the part of the pilot cannot said to be justified at all
since according to Capt. Mehta ifa pilot goes directly outbound
without reporting overhead VOR, he cannot maintain the correct
reference of time from VOR.

It may also be mentioned that at 07: 14:39 ATCO had asked
the aircraft to intimate its allitude at that time, to which the air -

A question arises why the flight crew deviated so much from
the laid down ILS let down procedure. The oily possible explana-
tion could be that since the aircraft did not come overhead VOR, it
had no reference with which it could measure the time of outbound
leg, fly on the outbound radial of 217 and gctting the outer marker
and middle marker signals. Since he did not lose the required
2,000 feel over VOR and did not fly for (wo and a half minutes on
the oulbound leg, he was unable to descend to an altitude of 5,000
feel at the end of the outbound Ieg.

Itis also relevant to note that the ATCO had asked the air-
craft at 07:11:37 UTC and also at 07:12:19 UTC to give a call while
commencing procedure turn. When the ATCO again asked the
aircraft at 07:14:48 to give a call conumencing procedure turn, it
inunediately reported that it was in the procedure turn. The FDR
dala shows that they had just commenced the procedure turn at

While carrying out ILS let down the aircraft is expected to go
fo a maximiun distance of about 13 nautical miles front VOR. The
Metcorological Department of India has futormied that oi the day of
the accident, the upper wind at 10,000 Icet allitude at Imphal was
aboul 10 kuots in a direction from 120° which is almost a cross
wind and would not alter the aircraft position with respect to VOR
during the outbound leg or in the procedure turn. It is, however,
to be noted here that the aircraft when it commenced its procedure
turn, was at a distance of about 17 nautical miles from VOR and
during the course of the procedure turn it went to a distance of
about 20.5 nautical miles from VOR where it lunpacted with the
hill. Obviously the aircraft was ala position where it should not


have been if the laid down ILS procedure was followed. The main
reasou as to why the aircraft went out so much away trom VOR, Is
that it did not go overhead VOR and commenced right (arn for
direct outbound nearly 14 miles away from the VOR and thereafter
it could not adhere to the ILS procedure in any manner. As the
aircraft was execuling procedure turn much away from ils normal
position, the normal terrains clearance was not available in thal
arca resulting in direct impact with the hill. The weather prevail-
ing al that Ume in that region was cloudy and it is most likely that
the crew could not have seen the hill ahead prior to impact as
- there ts no intra-cockpit conversalion indicating any auxicly or
emergency during the last phase of the flight. The GPWS warning
came ‘ON’ about six seconds prior to the fmipact when the alreralt
caie over the hilly terrain and one of the conditions for triggering
GPWS warning, nanicly, excessive terrain closer rate was met. The
CVR transcript and Uae FDR data do nol indicate Wat the flight
crew reacted immediaicly to the GPWS warning (hough however,
there was a gain of about 40 feet in the aircraft allitude in (he final
few seconds indicating that perhaps the crew had initiated go
around aclion. The terrain avoidance study carried out at Boeing
facilities and the simulator exercises carried out at CTE Hydera-
bad, however, showed that even if the crew had reacted immediate-
ly to the GPWS warning it was not possible for the aircraft to clear
the hill ahead. It is however to be noted that the GPWS is not
designed to provide a timely warning while approaching a steep
hill.

At Imphal whilst the ope Court hearing was taking place,
one Mr. Irengbam Boyaisingh being the sen of one lrengbam

such a doubt has crept in as to whether, in fact, there was any

that at no stage the Air Traffic Controller had asked the aircraft to
delay landing nor the commander had sought permission from ATC
for landing or any emergency landing. Therefore, the question of
ATC delaying the permission to land does nol arise neither there
was any extension of the outbound leg of the aircraft by reason of
delayed permission.

Iu the premises it can thus safely be concluded that the Pilot
has not only deviated from the Hight plan but also nol followed the
ILS let down chart. In order, however, to ascertain as to whether,

parlcular pilot, this Court directed the Indian Airlines to provide

as Ue Pilot-in-Command for Calcutta/Imphal route. In pursuance

It was suggested by both the Indian Airlines as also by the ICPA

unable to accept such a contention since there is no evidence on

Siluation viz. avoiding overhead Agartala and taking more or less a
direct route to Imphal with more or less again the same right turn
and then Jand without coming overhead VOR. It is in evidence by

v) Role of Co-pilot during the flight :

With the advancement of aviation technology and introduc-
tion of fly-by-wire control system, the role of Co-pilols has become
increasingly important in the present age. Co-pilot is not sup-
posed to be a mere passive and mute spectator in the cockpit bul
has a definile role to play in the matter of sale Hying of the atrcralt.

‘

124


The training progranune of the operator (Indian Airlines) also
recopnises such fmporlance and by reason wheretor co pilots are
(rained in the same quanner as (he PHot-dn-Comunand. Capt, M.V.V
Rao being the Operations Manager, Training, at CTE, Hyderabad,
in no uncertain terins stated that the co-pilot should be able to
meet all emergencies - the difference however being between the
Pilot and the Co-pilot is that while the former will be more profl-
cient the latter by reason of his lesser experience, may not be that
proficient.

Capl. M.V.V. Rao has also staled that it is one of the primary
duties of the Co-pilot to give corrective advice lo his Pilol-in-
Comman@ and in the event of lis failure to give such corrective
advice, hus efliciency cannot bul be doubled. Capt, Rao has pone
on furthd} to state that if the PHlot-in-Command makes a mistake,
it should Inot go unnoUced by lds Co-pilot and as a tmatter of Lact
both the Pilot and co-pilot should work as a tcam so that there is
no safety ‘hazard. It ts the definite evidence of Capt. Rao that iu
the eventlof deviations by the Captain, the co-pilot should caution
him and failure to administer such caution can be termed to be an
act of indiscipline and if it comes to light, steps would be taken
against Ue concerned Co-pilot. It is also the duty of the Co-pilot to
inform the management in the event of his experiencing any devia-
tions on the part of the Pilot-in-Comumand and as a matter of fact
there are some such instances, since Co-pilot is always aware of
the actions of the Pilot-in-Command during the course of the flight.

Incidentally i is to be noted that in the instant investigation,
a perusal of Cockpit Voice Recorder transmission docs not show
any word of caution or any correction to the deviations ol the Pilot-
in-Conunand by the Co-pilot. As a imatticr of fact, the Co-pilot was
as much’a party to the deviations as the Pilot-in-Comimand -
admittedly the flight crew were working as a team and both of
them were, therefore, deviating from the normal procedure as
depicted in the operational flight plan as also the ILS let down
chart. Equal indiscipline and lack of professionalisin, therefore,
can be ascribed so far as the Co-pilot is also concerned.

ra
N
ws

It is not out of place to record that the Operations Manual of
Indian Alrlines provide that the Co-pilot should monttor tle ina.
strument approach and il is especially tmportant Lhat thie Co-pilot
will automatically inform the Pilot-in-Command of any abnorimal
devialions from the approach procedure, altitude, rate of descent,
speed and timings. As a matter of fact, the Operations Manual
provides that Co-pilot during the approach acts as a safety pilot
and ought not to hesitate to inform the Pilot-in-Conunaud of any
abnormality or procedural discrepancy.

Considering the flight in question (iC-257), the deviations
right after Comilla continued till upto the crash point - not re-
porting overhead VOR Agartala and following an unspecified route
from overhead Comilla, starUng of the descent too early coutrary
to the operational flight plan; not reporting overhead VOR huophatl:
turning right much shorl of VOR al liphal; wrong altitude call-
outs at different locations, have all been accepted by the Co-pilot
without any caution or even an advice. This indicates that the Co-
pilot did not perform his duty as was expected of him. in terms of
the Operations Manual of Indian Airlines.

vi) Possibility of wrong Indication by VOR

During the course of investigation of this accident and al the
open hearings the operator raised doubt in regard Lo the mial-
functioning of the VOR being one of the navigational ald at (he
Imphal Airport. It was submitted that the Pilot-in-Comnimand has
iniliated a right (urn before coming overhead VOR, may be by
reason of a false bidication of the VOR. Capt. Mehta, however, tnt
no uncertain terms has negatived that suggestion. In this context,
particular reference may be had to queslion No. 194 and its answer
given by Capt. Mehta. The question as above together with the
answer is set out hereinbelow :-

"Q.194 : So, if you get a false indication on your VOR that can
easily be checked with the help of olher available

instrument on the cockpit itself? / Yes, cockpit has no
problem: to differentiate false signs".

Capt. Mechta clarified that mal-functionuing of VOR can easily
be checked in reference to other front pancl instruments. He
stated that it is a standard procedure that as soon as thie flag
comes in view on the instrument, the pilots check it with other
instruments. He stated thal question of being misguided by
reason of false VOR indication does not and cannot arise. Fur-
thermore, in the normal course of events, in the event of any dilfi-
culty being experienced by the pilot by reason of mal-functioning of
anyone of the cockpit instruments including VOR, there would
have been some discussions amongst the pilots and also a subse-
quent transmission in regard thereto to the Air Traffte controller.
On a perusal of the CVR transcrip{, no such inconvenience has
been discussed or transmilted to the ATCO. In fine, therefore. il
can safely be concluded that question of there being any mal-fune-
tioning of any panel instruments which could have mislead the
Pilot-in-Command does not and cannot arise.

Incidentally il is to be noted that on the fateful day two other
flights of Indian Airlines, nanicly, IC-890 from Delhi to Imphal was
operated by an Airbus A-320 aircraft and IC-255 from Calcutta to
Imphal operated by a Bocing 737 aircralt did, in fact, use all the
ground aids without any inconvenience whatsoever. The Com-
mander of 1C-255 (Capt. K. Patel) in his statement dated 220d
August, 1991, stated that all the navigational aids at lmiphal were
serviceable and were working normally. Capt. L.P. Mishra, who
opcrated the {light [C-890 has also conlirmed such a state of at-
fairs in regard to the navigational aids at lmphat Airport.

It is to be further noted that the VOR was calibrated by the
National Airports Authority aircraft by carrying out flight tests on
4th June, 1991 and its operational status was declared unrestrict-
ed. Immediately after the accident, the Director Gencral of Civil
Aviation ordered special flight check of (he VOR and other ground


aids, in pursuance whereof the ground aids at Imphal were cali-
brated on 18th August, LOOT, with a DGCA representillve for the
purpose of ascertaining as lo whether the factlides at Iniphal were
in normal working condition. The flight test ensured that all
paranicters of VOR were within the specified limits. In this con-
text, reference may be made to Annexure 'G' to this report wherein
it has been stated - “All parameters have been found to be within
defined limits. VOR let down procedure checked and found that
the indications were salisfactory and flyable".

Scrutiny of the maiulenance records of hnphal VOR indicat-
ed that maintenance personnel have been taking periodic meter
readings of the VOR equipment as well as undertaking monthly
ground checks. The last fault as recorded in the Fault Log Book of
the VOR occurred on 9th June, 1991, which was attended to and
rectified on the self same day. Thereafter, no faull has been re-
corded for this particular facillly (VOR) at Imphal Airport.

In the premises, in my view, it can safely be concluded that
question of there being any false indicalion of VOR does not arise
neither there is any scope for the pilot being misled by reason ofa
false indication.

vil) GPWS warning and Crew action :

CVR recordings have shown that GPWS warning lad (rig-
gered about six seconds prior lo the impact of the alreraft with the
hill. The FDR Data indicates that the aircrafi was descending ull
ihe time GPWS warning had come ON and thereafter the aircrat
alinost levelled off and it had started gaining height thereafter
indicating as if the Commander had initiated action lo climb up.

The evidence available shows that the aircraft impacted the
hill at an altitude of about 5200 fect and the maximuin height of
the hill at the point of impact is about 5500 feet indicating thereby
that the aircraft fmpacted about 300 [cet below the peak of the hill.


A question arose whether the aireratt could huawe cleared the
hill if action was initiated by the Pilot to ‘'Go-around! iinmediately
when the GPWS warning had come ‘ON’. In order to examine this
aspecl, simulator flights were carried out on B-737 simulator of
Indian Airlines at CTE Hyderabad using the CVR and FDR Data.
During the simulator flights the aircraft speed was maintained
around 180 kts. which was the speed at which the aircraft had
impacted. Since the aircraft was turning at an average rate of
about 3 degrees per second during the period when GPWS warning
was ‘ON’, corresponding 30 degree aircraft bank was maiutaliucd
during the simulator flight. As per the CVR read out the crew had
taken the Flaps to position 1. During the procedure (am generally
Flaps are at position 5. The simulator flighis were, therelore,
carried out for both Flap 1 and Flap 5 positions.

The simulator flights were carried out by Capt. DeCosta, a B-
737 Examiner along with Capt. R.I. Singh under the supervision of
Capt. Thergaonker. These flights were conducted in the presence
of the Court, DGCA, Dy. DGCA and Director of Air Safely. From
the simulator flights, it was observed that cven if inunediate action
was taken to pull up the aircraft and engine throttles moved
forward immediately when the GPWS warning had come ‘ON’, the
aircraft could gain a height of 100 to 110 [cet with Flaps 1 and
Flaps 5 respectively. It, therefore, indicates that even if the crew
had initiated inmmediate ‘Go-around’ when Che warning came "ON

the aircraft could not have cleared the hill.

Since the issue pertain to the pilot's reaction and has thus a
serious effect on the training aspect, I directed the Deputy Director
General of Civil Aviation, Government of India, to have a sinulator
exercise being done at the manufacturers end for terrain avoid-
ance. In accordance therewith, all necessary details were sent to
the manufacturers, and in fact, one Mr. Jaines Kerrigen, an Acro-
nautical Engineer of the Boeing Company from Seatile, Washing-
ton D.C. appeared before the Court of Inquiry as a witness with all


necessary evidence including the computerised graph for the
simulator exercise in regard to terrain avoidance. Mr. Kerrigen
slated Uhat wilh a moderate human approach to the sigual from
GPWS, the pilot could not have cleared the lerrain. Since taking
the pilot's reaction into consideration, the climb rate would be
around 90 feet per second after obtaining full throttle powcr and to
achieve full throttle power, the aircraft takes about 3.5 seconds. In
answer to a pointed question as to whether the climb rate would be
grealer after altainment of climb rate of first 90 feet per second,
Mr. Kerrigen stated that it would be again in the region of 90 feet
per sccond though there might be slight variation by reason of flap
position being flap 1 and flap 5, though however, variation would
be extremely negligible. It is to be noted, however, that originally
the simulation al the manufacturers end was carricd out on ‘Nil
Wind’ basis, but at the request of the Court special messages were
scl to the Bocing Company by Mr. Kerrigen for drawing out the
flight path on the basis of 10 and 20 knots of tail wind on the
outbound leg and Mr. kerrigen also produced the fax in regard
thereto, which however, does not show much of a difference in the
ground path by reason of the existence of the tail wind as above.
The study of the Boeing Company is already an annexure to thus
report being Annexure 'F’.

Though the aforesaid stucy indicates that ‘Go-round’ action
by crew after the warning had come ‘ON’ would not lave prevented
the accident, importance of Iminediate crew action whenever GPWS
warning comes ‘ON’ cannot be over-emphasised. When the aircratl
is flying in IMC conditions, the crew is not In a postion to deter-
miuine how close they are wilh the terrain and hence, immediate
action when GPWS warning comes ‘ON’ could save collision of
aircraft with terrain. Even when the aircraft is fying in VMC
conditions and crew may see the terrain the warning should not be
ignored since the aircraft may nol be in a safe configuration to land
i.e. the Landing Gear may nol be extended, flaps may not be in
landing configuration or the deviation from the ILS Glide Path may

be excessive. Even if crew could visually see that there is no

130


danger of collision with terrain, it is necessary to take lmmediate
action to cheek the alrcrafe configuration whenever GPWS warutig
comes ‘ON’. While Indian Airlines tave iiformed Chat their Craudniiiag
procedures require that GPWS warning should not be ignored and
immicdiate action should be taken, it is absolutely essential that
the drill of taking tnunediate action to ‘Go-around’ tn IMC condition
and to check the aircraft configuration in VMC condition should be
rigorously practiced during the training and subsequent refresher /
proficiency training and checks. The Airline should monitor this
aspect very closely so that crew react to the warning wilhout loss

of time which is very crucial in such situations.

While it is rue thal the method and mode of training seem
lo be rather rigorous and according to Capt. M.V.V. Rao in accord-
ance wilh olher global standard, but | have certain reservations in
regard to the career pattern of pilots in Indian Airlines. It is no-
(iced that young boys are recruited from open market and alter
training Uaey operate the flight as Co-pilol (P-2) in B-737 aircraft
from where, however, the same man progresses as Co-plilot om A-
320 aircrall and finally as Co-pilot on A-300 aircrall. Surprisiugly,
however, in terms of career pattern followed by Indian Airlines as
regards command endorsement jt is only P-2 in A-300 who gets a
conunaud endorsement as P-1 in B-737 and progressively as Pilot-
in-Command in A-320 and thereafter on A-300. This particular
career pattern cannot in my view stand to airy reason whatsoever
since from the conventional type (B-737) one is taken to fly-by-wire
glass cockpit as a Co-pilot and thereafter again Wransmitted to a
conventional aircraft (A-300) and finally transmitted back as Pilot-
in-Command in the conventional B-737 and thereafler from
conventional lo glass cockpit and then again lo conventional
alrcralt. It seems before drawing out Us carcer pattern there was
no strict application of mind so far as the operator (Indian Airlines)
is concerned otherwise (here would not have been a shift from
conventional to glass cockpit and from glass cockpit to conveition-
al aircraft on more than one occasion in the career of a particular
individual while acting as a Co-pilot and a Pilot-in-Command. In
my considered opinion this cannot be termed to be a very safe
career patlern and if this particular career pattern is continued |
have no hesitation to record that there will be nothing but a
compronise with the safely of air passengers. Tinie has now come


up, therefore, for the operator to restructure the whole career
pattern of the pilots so as (o be in conformity with Che salety dorms
as is prevalent Unroughout the globe. It is lo be noted, however,
that A-320 pilots (P-1 or P-2) do not fly any oUher aircraft in actu-
ality.

On the basis of available evidence, however, as regards Capt.
Halder's proficiency, | find that his Bocing 737 endorsement train-
ing was first taken up by Capt. M.S. Sharma, bul after about 2
hours and 15 minutes of simulator sorties, Capt. Sharma did not
find Capt. Halder to be suitable enough for undergoing the training
and he discontinued his training thereafter. Capt. M.V.V. Rao
upon proper scrutiny of the records maintained by the luadian
Airlines training institute, has stated that since Capt. Halder was
doing hard landings, Capt. Sharma discontinucd his training.
According lo Capt. Rao, this disconUnuance may be duc fo
comniuication gap between the traince and the instructor or
Capt. Halder’s performance being not upto the expected standards,
though however, there is no evidence available on U1is score and
the statement as above admittedly was purely on guess work of
Capt. Rao. Incidentally it is to be noted that after Capt. Sharma
discontinued with the training, Capt. Rahut was eutrusted for
training of Capt. Halder for Boeing endorsement wherein after 15
days Capt. Rahut found him fit for endorsement. It is not out of
context to note here, however, that Capt. Rahut also informed that
during his 15 days training, he found Capt. Halder coming high on
approach on turn which he corrected after five or six landings
Including the corrections of the defictencies reported by Capt.
Sharia.

On the basis of above, it therefore, appears that both Capt.
Sharma and Capt. Rahut found him deficient at the first stages on
two specific counts and as such a-doubt creeps in my mind as to
whether Capt. Halder should have been cleared for Boeing 737
endorsement without being taken up for further training so as to
give lim more cxpericnce or not. The trainer apparently felt that

there is no deficicncy in Capt. Halder. It is on this count that I
wish to pul on record that training even as per the procedure as
noted above ought to be in strict accordance therewith and there
ought not to be any laxity or any compromise wiih such a waining
toa pilot. Itis true that Capt. Sharma did not record that Capt.
Halder needs further experience before obtaining Bocing 737
endorsement, but omission to record the same ought not to be
taken note of as his assent to a new instructor to obtain Bocing
737 endorsement training.

In this perspective, therefore, |] would Hke to record my
observations that in the event of any deficiency being noticed by
(he trainer, tastead of changing the trainer further experience
ought to be given to the pilot concerned so that when he takes up
the course for endorsement, there cannot be any deficiency in the
pilot concerned. Let it be clarified that I am not trying to impute
any deficlency in Capt. Halder, but the system ought to be such so
as lo obviate such deficiencies during the endorsement training
course. This observation of nue is based mainly on the evidence
of Capt. Rao that in the normal course of events one instructor is
altached to one trainee unless the instructor is not available for
conUnuation of such training.

It is al this juncture thal my visil to Australia and Singapoie
secins to be well-worth. The training standards sel up by the
operator (Indian Airlines) and the Director General of Civil Aviation
cannot be termed to be different or deficient in any way from that
of the Qanlas or Singapore Airlines training programme. Asa
mnatter of fact, the training programme in our country Is as rigor-
ous as that of the other two countries but the system of training is
the sphere where we need focus our attention. Qantas Airlines has
evolved a course intiluled "Cockpit Resource Management Course"
(CRM). In the introduction to the CRM course, it is stated -
“perhaps the first, aud most identifiable element in Cockpit Re-
source Management training is that of using the available re-

sources jin decision making. One purpose of the CRM course is to

i

overconie a basic weakness that initial raining has bred into pi-
lois. Pilots have been taught to Uy aircraft as sole crew members.
CRM will teach us to use all the resources al our disposal to gather

develop solutions, implement the decision and evaluate (he per-

Another part of CRM training is understanding conmiunica-

produce the best results in specific situations. We will learn about

As part of the understanding of communication, we will learn

According to Qantas it is not a training department plot to
impose another series of discussion ilems for licence renewal. The
course will have no examination or tests aud the ongolng training
will 10t involve CRM test, the command prorogative will be main-
tained. The object is to assist the Captain lo come to the best

decision inaking process. The training is designed to leach them
when to speak, how to speak and if it is absolutely necessary
where not to take ‘no’ for an auswer. The idea is to practice CRM

and then to discuss the collective performance at a debricfing al


which the instructor is little more than the master of ceremonies.
To aid in the debriefing, the simulators are fitted with video came

consis(s of various factors, namely, (i) Decision process; (it) Situa-
Ulonal factors affecting decision making; (i) Behavioural deci-

team works. This CRM provides a better opportunity and an
understanding of the whole situation wluch would otherwise be nol
available to a flight crew while operating a flight.

Unfortunately, not an iota of evidence is available on record
before this Court of Inquiry in regard to this particular course of

The video play-back has a tremendous effect on human psychology
more so when the person viewing it, is a pilot. Nobody need tell
him about the draw-backs, if there be any, since one can realise
his own mistake through observation by himsclf and the operator
would be well-advised to introduce such a course for the purpose
of an effective cockpit management during the course of a flight.

As regards the Civil Aviation Authority, it is noliced in Aus-
tralia that there is no active participation of the regulatory authori-
ty, bul its functions are restricted to supervisory nalure since the
entire safety aspect of an air travel is dealt with in an elaborate

As regards the proficiency checks, the CAA Australla re-

operalor, namely, the Qantas Airways does the same checks them-

As regards the check pilots, it is the operator (Qantas Air-
ways) who nominates the check pilots upon completion of the

grants necessary certification in regard thereto afler the nonuuated

withdrawn forthwith.

As regards the inilial training, it appears that the standard
of pilots proficiency is carefully controlled by proper selection at
the inilial slage. Qantas carry out psychological tests and aptitude
tests for proper coordination and reaction in a given situation. The
ab-initio pilots are selected at an age of 19 years and it is the

for which a substantial amount of money is to be paid to the train-

trainees acquire Commercial Pilots’ Licence. Thereafter, the train-
ec undergoes 8 months training with Qantas on a particular type

years approximatcly lo be a first officer from he rank of second

conunand training and checks the first officer obtatus Pilot-in-
Coninaid endorsement. The Fight Operation Standards pilots
about 5 in number, carry out surveillance checks of about one per
cent of the sectors which is about 25 sectors per month. The
surveillance and monitoring is now being gradually delegated by
the Civil Aviation Authority to the airline Flight Operations Stand-

ards. ‘The Flight Operations Standards pilots monitor the standard
of individual pilots - they check the check pilots, tuspect the
simulators and training Instilutes and maintain data base of pllots.

As noted above, though the CAA requires that proficiency
checks of pilots be carried out al least twice evcry year. Qantas
carrics out such checks every three months and every pilot goes
through simulator exercise every three months. A report is pre-
pared every Ume and the assessment Is kept in the computer and
retrieved for review whenever the pilot goes for promotional train-

check, a pilot fails, he is taken off fying jmumediately tll he passes
the checks. The general trend of the proficiency of the pilots is
retrieved from the computer data which may: show any deficiency
in the (raining system if it prevails. Such monitoring reduces the
failure rales and hence saves Wraining costs. The nil accident rate
of Qantas has helped them in reducing the insuraiice cost and has
improved the passenger confideice as a result of which they get
higher share of passenger traffic. While appointing check pilots,
the computer data of the pilot is scrulinised by the Flight Stand-
ards branch and it is only thereafter a pilot is taken for training as
a check pilot. After successful completion of checks by the Flight
Standards pilots, the name of the pilot is recommended to CAA tor

approval.

Onc of the most important feature of monitoring aud surveil-
lance by Qantas is monitoring of Quick Access Recorder (QAR).
They monitor the data of every fight with the help of computer
system and analyse the 50 selected parameters to find out if there
exists any deviation from the limit values. If there isa general
trend of exceedence of any particular parameter the training
division is immediately informed to find out if there is any
deficiency in the training. On implementation of remedial
measures they have been able to drastically reduce the deviation
rates. The QAR analysis has been established with the
concurrence of pilots’ Union on the condition that the name of the


crew will not be divulged and any deviation observed during the

eliminate the same. The Qantas do nol take any direct action and
it is lef{ to the Unions. They have found the system working
salisfactorily as according to them the Unions and the individual
pilots are made morc responsille to ensure that 10 deviations from

the laid down procedures occur.

During the training of pilots in the simulator, they make the
video tapes which they show subsequently to the pilots so that

they become aware of any deficiencies during their simulator train-

ing.

Any personality traits of pilots are not allowed to affect air -
line operations. Even if a pilot passes every proficiency lest but if
his personalily can cause difficulty in airline operations, he is nol
given command endorsement and he is kept only as a first officer.

In the introduction chapter of this report, I have already
delved into the role of Executive Pilots in Australia. Il is not also

to have a feel of the operations and also provides enough time to
them to devote on the aspects of planning and moniloring the

maiutain very close watch on day-to-day operations and can devote
enough time in office to handle the operational matters.

Singapore Airlines is also one of the safest airlines in the
world. There has been no accident to Singapore Airlines during
the last 30 years. The training progranune of Singapore airline is

quite similar to the programmes in India and Australia. The opera-

tor prepares the training programune of pilots and submits the
same to CAA for approval. During the training on simulator, the
aircraft flight path is plolled by computer and if a pilot is found not
reacling urgently in an emergency, the instructor would repeat the
exercise. On operators aircraft, he CAA flight inspectors check the
check-pitots and examiners of Singapore Airlines. The flight in-
spectors carry oul checks of the examiners and the check pilots
once in about two months. The airline examiners check the profi-
clency of pilots and the CAA ensures that the laid down system
works and they only do sample checks.

The airline decides on which type of aircraft a pilot will go for
training and there is no cholee of a pilot to decide which aircrait le
would go for training. A CPL holder becomes first officer after
about eight months training wilh the atrline. During the training
he can go as a second officer and acts as a supermunmiary pilot.
Before a Co-pilot can become a Pilot-in-Conunand, he should get
ALTP and acquire about 5,000 hours experience as a Co-pilot.
Thus, a Co-pilot would become a Pilot-in-conunand after about 10

years.

A pilot can fly only one type of aircraft al a time. Pilots
holding command endorsements on basic B-747-200 or 300 air-
craft cannot fly B-747-400 aircraft. Only check pilots aud examiin-
ers can fly two types of aircraft. Even cabin crew can fly only on
two types of aireralt.

The simulator training is iinparted by instructors who are
current on the type. Only recurrent training can be given by re-
tired pilots who are appointed on ad hoc basis. Singapore Airlines
requires proficiency checks of pilots on simulator to be done at
every three months.

From the study of the training and proficicucy monitoring of
pilots by Qantas and Singapore Airlines, it is noted that the airline
systcins are well laid down, followed and monitored. It is (he

airlines which takes responsibility to ensure compliance of the laid
down procedures. The CAA is there to carry out random checks.
The cullure in the airline is so safely conscious thal one cannol
think that there would be any deviation from the laid down proce-
dures. The pilots and the associations are made responsible lo
remove deficiencies observed during the monitoring. The airline,
let alone the CAA, does not have to resort to punitive actions to
enforce compliance of the operating procedures. Such a culture
needs to be developed, if we have to take a major step forward
towards the safety of aircraft operaUions. All concerned whether au
individual pilot, pilots associations, the executive pilots, the
management of the airlines and the regulatory authorilics have to
re-orient and restructure their policies and prograniunes lo culti-
vate a healthy atmosphere, the spiril of teant work and colleclive
sharing of the responsibilitics.

The other aspect of the training to pilots in the matter of
terrain avoidance in our country ought also to be emphasised. It is
to be noted that the Aviation Research and Education Foundation —
of the United States of America recommended to the Federal Avia-
tion Authority in the context of Delayed GPWS Response Syndrome
the following :

- emphasize that the only acceptable procedure
for reacting to a GPWS pull-up warning of impending
impact with the ground is to inumediately set maxi-
mum thrust and rotate to the pitch augle for maxi-
nian angle of climb;

- specify that daylight visual observaUion is the
only acceptable means for contradicting such a GPWS
pull-up warning for terrain closure;

- ensure that the airplane flight manuals and
training curricula of all GPWS equipped U.S airHnes
prescribe the aforementioned procedures for dealing
with a terrain-closure GPWS pull-up warning;

- require that GPWS equipped U.S. carriers tn-

clude a terrain closure GPWS pull-up warning iu all
initial and recurrent simulator training sessions;

145


- seta deadiine for upgrading all firsi-gencratlon
GPWS equipment to the current state of the art; and

- ensure that the miuininium terrain clearance
standards used by GPWS are compatible with those
used in establishing minimum altitudes for radar
vectors and for approach charts.”

Subsequently, however, National Transportation Safety
Board of USA also in the line as above recommended that "FAA
should tustruct all air carriers to Include in their flightcrew proce-
dures instructions which require an immediate response to the
ground proximily system's terrain closure "pull-up" warning when
proximity to the terrain cannot be verified instantly by visual
observation. The required response to this warning should be that
the maximum avatlable thrust be applied and that the aircraft be
rotated lo achieve the best angle of climb without delay."

After considerable deliberation, (he Federal Aviation Admin-
istration, USA, also responded to the need of the hour and imple-
mented the reconunendations.

Both in Australia and in Singapore, the pilots are trained to
react immediately with the triggering of the Ground Proximity
Warning System. In Australla, during (raining of pilots lor
command endorsements, random GPWS warning is injected on to
the computer at the siniulator and the pilot's reaction is noled by
the computer. Such areaction thereafter is scrulinised and if
thought fit, further training is imparted on to the pilot in regard
thereto. The importance of adhering to GPWS is made known to
the Pilot-In-Command. Similar is the situation in Singapore.

It is heartening to note also the Director General of Civil
Aviation In his anxicty in regard to this parUcular branch of Air
Safety issued an Air Safety Circular in January, 1992 to the follow-
ing effect :-

SUBJECT: GROUND PROXIMITY WARNING SYSTEM RESPONSE

In the last two decades, the world over, it has been
noticed that there is a tendency on the part of the pilots
to ignore Ground Proximity Warning resulting in avoid-
able accidents. It has been the cause of worry and

146


Ag

concern amongst the Civil Aviation Authorities the
world over. Circulars have been issued by FAA as early
as 1981 in regard to procedures to be followed when a
ground proxtmity warning is sounded in the cockpit of
the aircraft.

Pilots have a tendency to ignore ground proximity
warning, based on their experience of false triggering of
this alarm. When they had responded to the alarm in
other causes, it had been too late. In India also, it has
also been observed in a few cases, that pilots have
ignored Ground Proximity Warning with disastrous
consequences.

It is, therefore, hereby ordered that the following
procedures may be followed by all pilots :

DQ In regard to terrain closure "pull up" alarm when
the pilot has no accurate visual observation as a
basis for contradicting it, the moment the alarm
is sounded, the pilot may execute corrective action
by immediate application of maximum available
thrust while simultaneously rotating the airplane
to achieve the best angle of climb. The rate of
rotation must be consistent with the existing
Slight parameters of the aircraft concerned so as
not to exceed the critical angle of attack.

ti) Pilots should not inhibit GPWS warning system
unless there is positive contra indications by
accurate visual observation.

ii) Air carriers should review their operations
manuals to incorporate appropriate flight crew
training procedures to train pilots for action with
respect to GPWS signals. The airline carriers may
incorporate even in the training programmes
appropriate routines for training, to respond to
GPWS alarm. They should also test the pilots to
such GPWS response during checks, trainings and
other routine exercises which they are required to
carry out in the simulator from time to time.

iv) Airlines may immediately carry out a check of all
the GPWS equipment on the aircraft to ensure
their integrity and proper serviceabllity to elimi-
nate false alarm.

1.
2.
3.

Action taken by the Airlines in compliance of this direc-
tive that :

(a) they have brought this circular to the notice of all
their flying crews;

tb) they have incorporated suitable provisions in their
operations manuals; and

(c) they have issued appropriate direction to the
Training Establishments for the training proce-
dures required in this regard.

be reported to the DGCA by 25.1.1992.

mation of Indian Airlines is concerned, the saine is quoted herein-

crew through our Regional Operalions Manager.

para 8.5.0 page 8.26 of [A Operations Manual VoL...

{c) Direclor of Training, CTE, Hyderabad has been advised
for faking sultable action vide our letter No HOP /23-
7863/5232 dated 20th January, 1992."

Iam, however, constrained to note here that even though the
Operalions Manager (Training), was available in the witness box,
the implementation by the Director of Training in regard to the
circular issued by the Director General of Civil Aviation has not
been placed on record and as matter of fact on a pointed question
from the Court, the Operations Manager simply stated that their
training procedures require that GPWS warning should not be
ignored and imuuediate action should be taken. While theoretically
this may seen to be otherwise in accordance wilh the circular, but
there is some alount of difference between the theory and the
actual practical training course. No evidence whatsoever is avail-
able on record to show the introduction of a curriculum in regard
to the pilot's action on the triggering of the GPWS, neither their
training manual or a syllabus in regard to has been produced
before the Court - this state of affairs cannot be stated to be very
encouraging.

In imy view, it is high Ue that due importance be attached
lo the triggering of GPWS. The drill of taking tmmediale action to
go round tn IMC condition and to check the aircraft configuration
should be rigorously practiced during the training. Proper syllabus
be prepared in regard Uherelo and the instructors be directed to
treal this parUcular aspect as an independent subject of their
training and the proficiency checks should also take note of pilot's
reaction to the triggering of GPWS.
